Precedent	DAs J.-- Article 19 protects some of the important attributes of personal liber- ty as independent rights and the expression "personal liber- ty" is used in Art.
Precedent	21 as a compendious term 90 including within Rs meaning all varieties of rights which go to make up the personal liberties of men.
Precedent	<NAME> if it be assumed that Art.

Precedent	One of the objects of preventive detention is to restrain a person detained from moving from place to place so that he may not spread disaffection or indulge in dangerous activities in the places he visits.
Precedent	The same consideration applies to the cases of persons who are interned or externed.
Precedent	Hence, externment, interment and certain other forms of restric tion on movement have always been treated as kindred matters belonging to the same group or family, and the rule which applies to one must necessarily apply to the others.

RatioOfTheDecision	Per <NAME> <NAME> and <NAME> (FAZL ALI J. dis- senting).--In Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	9.1 the word 'law" has been used in the sense of <ORG>-made law and not as an equivalent of law in the abstract or general sense embodying the principles of natural justice; and "procedure established by law" means procedure established by law made by the <ORG>, that is to say, the Union <ORG> or the Legislatures of the <ORG>s.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not proper to construe this expression in the light of the meaning given to.the expression "due proc ess of law" in the American Constitution by <ORG>.
Precedent	<ORG> in Art.
Precedent	21 does not mean the jus naturale of civil law but means 91 positive or <ORG>-made law.
Precedent	"Procedure established by law" does not however mean any procedure which may be prescribed by a competent legislature, but the ordinary well-estab- lished criminal procedure, i.e., those settled.
Precedent	usages and normal modes of procedure sanctioned by the Criminal Proce- dure Code, which is the general law of criminal proce- dure in this country.
Precedent	The only alternative to this con- struction, if a constitutional transgression is to be avoid- ed is to interpret the reference to "law" as implying a constitutional 'amendment pro tanto, for it is only a law enacted by the procedure provided for such amendment that could modify or override a fundamental right without contra- vening Art.
Precedent	13 (2).
Precedent	<NAME>, <NAME> is nothing revolutionary in the view that "procedure established by law "must include the four principles of elem entary justice which inhere in and are at the root of all civilized systems of law, and which have been stated by the American Courts and jurists as consisting in (1) notice, (2) opportunity to be heard, (3) impartial tribunal and (4) orderly course of procedure.
RatioOfTheDecision	These four principles are really different aspects of the same right, namely, the right to be heard before one is condemned.
RatioOfTheDecision	Hence the words "procedure established by law ", whatever its exact meaning be, must necessarily include the principle that no person shall be condemned without hearing by an impartial tribunal.
RatioOfTheDecision	Per <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME> 3 of the Preventive Detention Act, 1950, does not delegate any legislative power to an executive officer but merely confers on such officer a discretion to enforce the law made by the legislature, and is not there- fore invalid on this ground.
RatioOfTheDecision	The fact that the section does not provide an objective standard for determining whether the requirements of law have been  complied with, is not a ground for holding that it is invalid.
Precedent	FAZL ALI J.---Section 3 is however a reasonable provision only for the first step, i.e., for arrest and initial detention and must be followed by some procedure for testing the so-called subjective satisfaction, which can be done only by providing a suitable machinery for examining the grounds on which the order of detention is made and considering the representations of the persons detained in relation to those grounds.
Precedent	Per KANIA C. J., <NAME> and DAS JJ.---Section 7 of the said Act is not invalid merely because it does not provide for an oral hearing or an opportunity to lead evidence but only gives right to make a representation.
Precedent	Right to an oral hearing and right to give evidence are not necessarily implied in the right to make a representation given by Art.
Precedent	22.

Precedent	11 that a person may be detained for such period as the 12-A 92 State thinks fit does not contravene Art.
Precedent	22 (7) and it  is not therefore invalid.

Precedent	JUDGMENT:  ORIGINAL JURISDICTION: Petition No.
Precedent	XIII of 1950.
Precedent	Application under Art.
Precedent	32 (1) of the Constitution of India for a writ of habeas corpus against the detention of the appellant in the Madras jail in pursuance of an order of detention made under the Preventive Detention Act, 1950.
Statute	The material facts of the case and arguments of counsel are set out in detail in the judgments.
Statute	The relevant provisions of the Preventive Detention Act, 1950, are printed below.
Statute	93  1.
Statute	Short title, extent and duration.--This Act may be called the Preventive Detention Act, 1950.
Statute	(2) It extends to the whole of India .....  (3) It shall cease to have effect on the 1st day of April, 1951, as respects things done or omitted to be done before that date.
Statute	2.
Statute	Definitions.--In this Act, unless the context other- wise requires,--  (a) "State Government" means, in relation to a Part C State, the Chief Commissioner of the State; and  (b) "detention order" means an order made under Section 3.
Statute	3.
Statute	Power to make orders deta ining certain persons.--(1) <ORG> or <ORG> may---  (a) if satisfied with respect to any person that with a view to preventing him from acting in any manner prejudicial to--  (i) the defence of India, the relations of India foreign power, or the security of India, or  (ii) the security of the <ORG> or the maintenance of public order, or  (iii) the maintenance of supplies and services to the community, or  (b) if satisfied with respect to any person who is a foreigner within the meaning of the Foreigners Act , 1946 (XXXI of 1946), that with a view to regulating his continued presence in India or with a view to making arrangements for his expulsion from India it is necessary so to do, make an order directing that such person be detained.
Statute	(2) Any District Magistrate or Sub-Divisional Magistrate, or Presidency-town, the Commissioner of Police, may, if satisfied provided in sub-clauses (ii) and (iii) of clause  (a) of sub-section (1), exercise the power conferred by the said sub-sec tion.
Statute	(3) When any order is made under this section by a Dis- trict Magistrate, Sub-Divisional Magistrate or Commissioner of Police, he shall forthwith report the fact to <ORG> to which he is subordinate together with the grounds on which the order has been made and such other particulars as in his opinion have a bearing on the necessi- ty for the order.
Statute	7.
Statute	Grounds of order of detention to be disclosed to persons affected by the order.--(1) When a person is de- tained in pursuance of a detention order, the authority making the order shall, as soon as may be, communicate to him the grounds on which the order his been made, and shall afford him the earliest opportunity of making a representa- tion against the order, in a case where such order has been made by <ORG>, to that <ORG>, and in a case where it has been made by a State <ORG> or an officer subordinate thereto, to <ORG>.
Statute	94  11.
Statute	Confirmation of detention order.--In any case where the Advisory Bo ard has reported that* there is in Rs opinion suffcient cause for the detention of the person concerned, <ORG> or <ORG>.
Statute	as the case may be, may confirm the detention order and continue the detention of the person concerned for' such period as it thinks fit.
Statute	12.
Statute	Duration of detention in certain cases.--(1) Any person detained in any of the following classes of cases or under 'my of the following circumstances may be detained without obtaining the opinion of <ORG> for a period longer than three months, but not exceeding one year from the date of his detention, namely, where such person has been detained wish a view to preventing him from acting in any manner prejudicial to--  (a) the defence of India, relations of India with foreign powers or the security- of India; or  (b) the security of a <ORG> or the maintenance of public order.
Fact	* * *  14.
Statute	Disclosure of grounds of detention, etc.--(1) No court shall, except for the purpose of a prosecution for an offence punisha ble under sub-section (9,), allow any state- ment to be made, or any evidence to be given.
Statute	before it of the substance of any communication made under section 7 of the grounds on which a detention order has been made against any person or of any representation made by 'him against such order; and notwithstanding anything contained in any other law, no court shall be entitled to require any public officer to produce before it, or to disclose the substance of, any such communication or representation made, or the proceedings of <ORG> or that par of the report of <ORG> which is confidential.
Statute	(2) It shall be an offence punishable with imprisonment for term which may extend to one year, or with fine, or with both, for any person to disclose or publish without the previous authorisation of <ORG> or <ORG>, as the case may be, any contents or matter purporting to be contents of any such communication or representation as is referred to in sub-section (1):  Provid ed that nothing in this sub-section shall apply to a disclosure made' to his legal adviser by a person who is the subject of a detention order.
Fact	<NAME> ( <CITATION_1286989> , with him) for the petitioner.
Fact	<NAME>, Advocate-General of Madras (<NAME> and <NAME>, with him) lot the State of Madras.
Fact	<NAME>, Attorney-General for India (<NAME>, with him) for <ORG>.
Fact	95  1950.
Fact	May 19.
Fact	The following Judgments were delivered.
Fact	<NAME>--This is a petition by the applicant under article 32 (1) of the Constitution of India for a writ of habeas corpus against his detention in the Madras Jail.
Fact	In the petition he has given various dates showing how he has been under detention since December, 1947.
Fact	Under the ordi- nary Criminal Law he was sentenced to terms of imprisonment but those convictions were set aside.
Argument	While he was tires under detention under one of the orders of <ORG>, on the 1st of March, 1950, he was served with an order made under se ction 3 (1) of the Preventive Detention Act, IV of 1950.
Argument	He challenges the legality of the order as it is contended that Act IV of 1950 contravenes the provisions of articles 13, 19 and 21 and the provisions of that Act are not in accordance with article 22 of the Con- stitution.
Argument	He has also challenged the validity of the order on the ground that it is issued <NAME> fide.
Argument	The burden of proving that allegation is on the applicant.
Argument	Because of the penal provisions of section 14 of the impugned Act the applicant has not disclosed the grounds, supplied to him, for his detention and the question of <NAME> fides of the order therefore cannot be gone into under this petition.
Fact	The question of the validity of Act IV of 1950 was argued before us at great length.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is the first case in which the different articles of the Constitution of India contained in the Chapter on Fundamental Rights has come for discussion before us.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Court is indebted to the learned counsel for the applicant and the Attorney-General for their as sistance in interpreting the true meaning of the relevant clauses of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	In order to appreciate the rival contentions it is useful first to bear in mind the general scheme of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under article 53 of the Constitution the executive power of the <ORG> is vested in the President and is to be exercised by him in accordance with the Constitution either directly or through officers subordinate to him.
RatioOfTheDecision	The legislative powers of the <ORG> are divided between the <ORG> and <ORG> of the States.
RatioOfTheDecision	The ambit and limitations on their respective powers are found in article 246 read with article 245 , Schedule VII, Lists 1,2 and 3 of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	For the <ORG> of India <ORG> is established and its powers and jurisdiction are set out in articles 124 to 147.
RatioOfTheDecision	This follows the pat- tern of the Government of India Act, 1935, which was the previous Constitution of the Government of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	Unlike the American Constitution, there is no article vesting the judicial power of the  <ORG> in <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The material points substantially altering the edifice are first in the Preamble which declares india a Sovereign Democratic Republic to secure to all its citizens justice, liberty and equality and to promote among them all, frater- nity.
RatioOfTheDecision	Part III of the Constitution is an important innova- tion.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is headed "Fundamental Rights."
RatioOfTheDecision	In that Part the word "State" includes both <ORG> and <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	By article 13 it is expressly provided that all laws in force in the territory of India, immediately before the commencement of the Constitution, in so far as they are inconsistent with the provisions of this Part, to the extent of such inconsistency, are void.
RatioOfTheDecision	There- fore, all laws in operation in India on the day the Consti- tution came into force, unless otherwise saved, to the extent they are inconsistent with this Chapter on Fundamen- tal Rights, become automatically void.
Statute	Under article 13 (2) provision is made for legislation after the  Constitution comes into operation.
Statute	It is there provided that the <ORG> shall not make any law which takes away or abridges the rights conferred by this Part and any law made in contraven- tion of this clause shall to the extent of the contraven- tion, be void.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, as regards future legislation also the Fundamental Rights in Part III have to be respected and, unless otherwise saved by the provisions of the Consti- tution, they will be void to the extent they contravene the provisions of Part III.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under article 245 (1) the legisla- tive powers conferred under  article 246 are also made "subject to the provisions of this Constitution," which of course includes Part III dealing with the Fundamental Rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	The term law in article 13 , is expressed to be wide enough to include Acts, Ordinances, Orders, Bye-laws, Rules, Regulations and even custom or usage having, in the territory of India, the force of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The rest of this Part is divided in seven divisions.
RatioOfTheDecision	"Right to Equality" is found in articles 14-18,  "Eight to Freedom" in articles 19-22, "Right against Exploitation" in articles 23 and 24, "Right to Freedom of Religion" in articles 25-28, "Cultural and Educational Rights" in articles 29 and 30, "Right to Property" in article 31 and "Right to Constitu- tional Remedies" in articles 32-35.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this case we are directly concerned only with the articles under the caption "Right to Freedom" (19-22) and article 32 which gives a remedy to enforce, the rights conferred by this Part.
RatioOfTheDecision	The rest of the articles may have to be referred to only to assist in the interpretation of the above-mentioned arti- cles.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is obvious that by the insertion of this Part the powers of the <ORG> and the <ORG>, both of the Union and the States, are further curtailed and the right to enforce the Fundamental Rights found in Part III by a direct application to <ORG> is removed from the legislative control.
Argument	The wording of article 32 shows that <ORG> can be moved to grant a suitable relief, mentioned in art icle 32 (2 ), only in respect of the Funda- mental Rights mentioned in Part III of the Constitution.
Argument	The petitioner is detained under a preventive detention order, made under Act IV of 1950, which has been passed by <ORG>.
Statute	In the Seventh Schedule of the Constitution, List I contains entries specifying items in respect of which the <ORG> has exclusive legislative powers.
Statute	Entry 9 is in these terms: "preventive detention for reasons connected with <ORG>, Foreign Affairs or the Security of India; persons subjected to such detention."
Statute	List III of that Schedule enumerates topics on which both the Union and the <ORG>s have concurrent legislative powers.
Statute	Entry 3 of that List is in these terms: "Preventive detention for reasons connected with the security of a <ORG>, the maintenance of public order or the maintenance of supplies and services essential to the community; persons subjected to such detention."
Statute	It is not disputed that Act IV of 1950 is covered by these two Entries in List I and Lis t III of the Seventh Schedule.
Statute	The contention of the peti- tioner is that the impugned legislation abridges or in- fringes the rights given by articles 19-21 and is also not in accordance with the permissive legislation on preventive detention allowed under articles 22 (4) and (7) and in particular is an infringement of the provisions of article 22 (5).
Statute	It is therefore necessary to consider in detail each of these articles and the arguments advanced in respect thereof.
Statute	Article 19 is for the protection of certain rights of freedom to citizens.
Statute	It runs as follows :--  "19.
Statute	(1)--All citizens shall have the right-(a) to free- dom of speech and expression;  (b) to assemble peaceably and without arms;  (c) to form associations or unions;  (d) to move freely throughout the territory of India;  (e) to reside and settle in any part of the territory of India;  (f) to acquire, hold and dispose of property; and  (g) to practise any profession, or to carry on any occupation, trade or business.
Statute	"(2) Nothing in sub-claus e (a) of clause (1) shall affect the operation of any existing law in so far as it relates to, or prevent the <ORG> from making any law relat- ing to, libel, slander, defamation, contempt of court or any matter which offends against decency or morality or which undermines the security of, or tends to overthrow, the <ORG>.
Statute	(3) Nothing in sub-clause (b) of the said clause shall affect the operation of any existing law in so far as it imposts, or prevent the <ORG> from making any law imposing, in the interests of public order reasonable restrictions on the exercise of the right con- ferred by the said sub-clause.
Statute	(4) Nothing in sub-clause (c) of the said clause shall affect the operation of any existing law in so far as it imposes, or prevent the <ORG> from making any law imposing, in the interests of public order or morality, reasonable restrictions on the exercise of the right conferred by the said sub-clause.
Statute	(5) Nothing in sub-clauses (d), (e) and (f) of the said clause shall affect the operation of any e xisting law in so far as it imposes, or prevent the <ORG> from making any law imposing, reasonable restrictions on the exercise of any of the rights conferred by the said sub-clauses either in the interests of the general public or for the protection of the interests of any Scheduled Tribe.
Statute	(6) Nothing in sub-clause (g) of the said clause shall affect the operation of any existing law in so far as it imposes, or prevent the <ORG> from making any law imposing, in the interests of the general public, reasonable restric- tions on the exercise of the right conferred by the said sub-clause, and, in particular, nothing in the said sub- clause shall affect the operation of any existing law in so far as it prescribes or empowers any authority to prescribe, or prevent the <ORG> from making any law prescribing or empowering any authority to prescribe, the professional or technical qualifications necessary for practising any pro- fession or carrying on any occupation, trade or business."
Statute	Clause (2) specifies the limit s up to which the abridge- ment- of the right contained in 19 (1) (a) may be permitted.
RatioOfTheDecision	it is an exception.
RatioOfTheDecision	Similarly clause (3) sets out the limit of abridgement of the right in 19 (1) (b) and clause (4) specifies such limits in respect of the right in 19 (1) (c).
RatioOfTheDecision	Clause (5) is in respect of the rights mentioned in 19 (1)  (d), (e) and (f) and clause (6) is in respect of the rights contained in 19 (1) (g).
RatioOfTheDecision	It cannot be disputed that the articles collected under the caption "Right to Freedom" have to be considered together to appreciate the extent of the Fundamental Rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the first place it is necessary to notice that there is a distinction between rights given to citizens and persons.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is clear on a perusal of the provisions of article 19 on the one hand and articles 20, 21 and 22 on the other.
RatioOfTheDecision	In order to determine whether a right is abridged or infringed it is first necessary to determine the extent of the right given by the articles and the limitations pre- scribed in the articles themselves perm itting its curtail- ment.
RatioOfTheDecision	The inclusion of article 13 (1) and (2) in the Con- stitution appears to be a matter of abundant caution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even in their absence, if any of the fundamental rights was infiringed by any legislative enactment, the <ORG> has always the power to declare the enactment, to the extent it transgresses the limits, invalid.
RatioOfTheDecision	The existence of article 13 (1) and (2) in the Constitution therefore is not material for the decision of the question what fundamental right is given and to what extent it is permitted to be abridged by the Constitution itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	As the preventive detention order results in the deten- tion of the applicant in a cell it was contended on his behalf that the rights specified in article 19 (1) (a ), (b ),  (c), (d), (e) and (g) have been infringed.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was argued that because of his detention he cannot have a free right to speech as and where he desired and the same argument was urged in respect of the rest of the rights mentioned in sub-clauses (b), (c), (d), (e) and (g).
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> gh this argu- ment is advanced in a case which deals with preventive detention, if correct, it-should be applicable in the case of punitive detention also to any one sentenced to a term of imprisonment under the relevant section of the Indian Penal Code .
RatioOfTheDecision	So considered, the argument must clearly be rejected.
RatioOfTheDecision	In spite of the saving clauses (2)to(6) permitting abridge- ment of the rights connected with each of them, punitive detention under several sections of the Penal Code , e.g., for theft, cheating, forgery and even ordinary assault, will be illegal.
RatioOfTheDecision	'Unless such conclusion necessarily follows from the article, it is obvious that such construction should be avoided.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion, such result is clearly not the outcome of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The article has to be read without any pre-conceived notions.
RatioOfTheDecision	So read, it clearly means that the legislation to be examined must be directly in respect of one of the rights mentioned in the subclauses.
RatioOfTheDecision	If there is a legislation directly attempting to control a citizen's f reedom of speech or expression, or his right to assemble peaceably and without arms, etc., the question whether that legislation is saved by the relevant saving clause of article 19 will arise.
RatioOfTheDecision	If, however, the legisla- tion is not directly in respect of any of these subjects, but as a result of the operation of other legislation, for instance, for punitive or preventive detention, his right under any of these subclauses is abridged, the question of the application of article 19 does not arise.
RatioOfTheDecision	The true approach is only to consider the directness of the legisla- tion and not what will be the result of the detention other- wise valid, on the mode of the detenue's life.
RatioOfTheDecision	On that short ground, in my opinion, this argument about the in- fringement of the rights mentioned in article 19 (1) gener- ally must fail.
RatioOfTheDecision	Any other construction put on the article, it seems to me.
Precedent	will be unreasonable.
Precedent	It was next urged that while this interpretation may meet the contention in respect of rights under article 19 (1) (a ), (b  ), (c ), (e) and (g ), the right given by article 19 (1) (d) is left untouched.
Precedent	That sub-clause expressly gives the right "to move freely throughout the territory of India."
Precedent	It was argued that by the confinement of the peti- tioner under the preventive detention order his right to move freely throughout the territory of India is directly abridged and therefore the <ORG> must show that the im- pugned legislation imposes only reasonable restrictions on the exercise of that right in the interests of the general public or for the protection of the interests of any Sched- uled Tribe, under article 19 (5).
Precedent	The Court is thus en- joined to inquire whether the restrictions imposed on the detained person are reasonable in the interests of the general public.
Precedent	Article 14 of the Constitution gives the right to equality in these terms:  "The <ORG> shall not deny to any person equality before the law or the equal protection of the laws within the territory of India."
Precedent	102  It was argued that the words "within the territory  of India" are unnecessary in that article because <ORG> is supreme to make laws operative only within the territory of India.
Precedent	Without those words also the article will bear the same meaning.
Precedent	Similarly, it was urged that the words "territory of India" in article 19 (1) (d) may be treated as superfluous, and preventive detention would thus be an abridgement of the right to move freely.
Precedent	In my opin- ion, this rule of construction itself is faulty.
RatioOfTheDecision	Because certain words may be considered superfluous (assuming them to be.
RatioOfTheDecision	so in article 14 for the present discussion) it is quite improper to assume that they are superfluous wherever found in the rest of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	On the contrary, in my opinion, reading sub-clause (d) as a whole the words "territory of India" are very important.
RatioOfTheDecision	What is sought to be protected by that sub-clause is the right to freedom of movement, i.e., without restriction, throughout the terri- tory of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	Read with their natural grammatical.. mean- ing the sub-clause only means  that if restrictions are sought to be put upon movement of a citizen from <ORG> to <ORG> or even within a <ORG> such restrictions will have to be tested by the permissive limits prescribed in clause (5) of that Article.
RatioOfTheDecision	Sub-clause (d) has nothing to do with detention, preventive or punitive.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution men- tions a right to freedom of movement throughout the territo- ry of India, Every word of that clause must be given its true and legitimate meaning and in the construction of a Statute, particularly a Constitution, it is improper.
RatioOfTheDecision	to omit any word which has a reasonable and proper place in it or to refrain from giving effect to its meaning.
RatioOfTheDecision	This position is made quite clear when clause (5) is read along with this sub-clause.
RatioOfTheDecision	It permits the imposition of reasona- ble.
RatioOfTheDecision	restrictions on the exercise of such right either in the interest of general public or the protection of the interest of any Scheduled Tribe.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is difficult to conceive of a reasonable restriction necessary in the interests of the genera l public for confining a person in a cell.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such restriction may be appropriate to prevent a person from going from one Province to another or one area to another, having regard to local conditions prevailing in particular areas.
RatioOfTheDecision	The point however is made abundantly clear by the alternative, viz., for the protec- tion of the interests of any Scheduled Tribe.
RatioOfTheDecision	What protec- tion of the interests of a Scheduled Tribe requires the confinement of a man in a cell ?
RatioOfTheDecision	On the other hand, pre- venting the movement of a person from one part of the terri- tory of India to another and the question of reasonable restriction imposed to protect the interests of a Scheduled Tribe is clearly intelligible and often noticed in the course of the administration of the country.
RatioOfTheDecision	Scheduled Tribes have certain rights, privileges and also disabili- ties.
RatioOfTheDecision	They have their own civilization, customs and mode of life and prevention of contact with persons or groups with a particular Scheduled Tribe may be considered undesirable during a certai n time or in certain conditions.
RatioOfTheDecision	The legis- lative history of India shows that Scheduled Tribes have been given a separate place on these grounds.
RatioOfTheDecision	Reading article 19 as a whole, therefore, it seems to me that it has no application to a legislation dealing with preventive or punitive detention as its direct object.
RatioOfTheDecision	I may point out that the acceptance of the petitioner's argument on the interpretation of this clause will result in the <ORG> being called upon to decide upon the reasonableness of several provisions of the Indian Penal Code and several other penal legislations as abridging this right.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even under clause (5), the <ORG> is permitted to apply the test of reasonable- ness of the restrictions or limits not generally, but only to the extent they are either in the interests of the gener- al public, e.g., in case of an epidemic, riot, etc., or for the protection of the interests of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion, this is not the intention of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore the contention urged in respect o f article 19 fails.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was argued that article 19 and article 21 should be read together as implementing each other.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 19 gave substantive rights to citizens while article 21 prescribed that no person can be deprived of his life and personal liberty except by procedure established by law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even so, on a true construction of article 19 , it seems to me that both preventive and punitive detention are outside the scope of article 19.
RatioOfTheDecision	In order to appreciate the true scope of article 19 it is useful to read it by itself and then to consider how far the other articles in Part HI affect or control its meaning.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is the first article under the caption "Right to Freedom ."
Precedent	It gives the rights mentioned in 19 (1) (a) to  (g) to all citizens of India.
Precedent	These rights read by them- selves and apart from the controls found in clauses (2) to (6) of the same article, specify the different general rights which a free citizen in a democratic country ordi- narily has.
Precedent	Having specified those rights, each of them is consider ed separately from the point of view of a similar right in the other citizens, and also after taking into consideration the principle that individual liberty must give way, to the extent it is necessary, when the good or safety of the people generally is concerned.
Precedent	Thus the right to freedom of speech and expression is given by 19 (1) (a).
Precedent	But clause (2) provides that such right shall not prevent the operation of a law which relates to libel, slander, defamation, contempt of <ORG> or any matter which offends against decency or morality or which undermines the security of, or tends to overthrow, the <ORG>.
Argument	Clause (2) thus only emphasizes that while the individual citizen has a free right of speech or expression, he cannot be permit- ted to use the same to the detriment of a similar right in another citizen or to the detriment of the <ORG>.
Argument	Thus, all laws of libel, slander, contempt of <ORG> or laws in respect of matters which offend against decency or morality are reaffirmed to be operative in spite of this ind ividual right of the citizen to freedom of speech and expression.
Argument	Simi- larly; that right is also subject to laws which prevent undermining the security of the <ORG> or against activities which tend to overthrow the <ORG>.
Argument	A similar analysis of clauses f3) and (4) shows similar restrictions imposed on similar grounds.
Argument	In the same way clause (5) also permits reasonable restrictions in the exercise of the right to freedom of movement throughout the territory of India, the right to reside and settle in any part of the territory of India or the right to acquire, hold and dispose of property, being imposed by law provided such reasonable restrictions on the exercise of such right are in the inter- est of the general' public.
Precedent	The Constitution further pro- vides by the same clause that similar reasonable restric- tions could be put on the exercise of those rights for the protection of the interest of a Scheduled Tribe.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is obviously to prevent an argument being advanced that while such restriction could be put i n the interest of general public, the Constitution did not provide for the imposi- tion of such restriction to protect the interests of a smaller group of people only.
RatioOfTheDecision	Reading article 19 in that way as a whole the only concept appears to be that the specified rights of a free citizen arc thus controlled by what the framers of the Constitution thought were necessary restric- tions in the interest of the rest of the citizens.
RatioOfTheDecision	Reading article 19 in that way it appears to me that the concept of the right to move freely throughout the territo- ry of India is an entirely different concept from the right to "personal liberty" contemplated by article 21.
RatioOfTheDecision	"Person- al liberty" covers many more rights in one sense and has a restricted meaning in another sense.
RatioOfTheDecision	For instance, while the right to move or reside may be covered by the expression ,'personal liberty" the right to freedom of speech (men- tioned in article 19 (1) (a)) or the right to acquire, hold or dispose of property (mentioned in 19 (1) (f)) cannot be conside red a part of the personal liberty of a citizen.
RatioOfTheDecision	They form part of the liberty of a citizen but the limita- tion imposed by the word "personal"leads me to believe that those rights are not covered by the expression personal liberty.
RatioOfTheDecision	So read there is no conflict between articles 19 and 21.
RatioOfTheDecision	The contents and subject matters of articles 19 and 21 are thus not the same and they proceed to deal with the rights covered by their respective words from totally different angles.
RatioOfTheDecision	As already mentioned in respect of each of the rights specified in sub-clauses of article 19 (1) specific limitations in respect of each is provided, while the expression "personal liberty" in article 21 is generally controlled by the gener- al expression "procedure established by law."
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitu- tion, in article 19 , and also in other articles in Part III, thus attempts to strike a balance between individ- ual liberty and the general interest of the society.
RatioOfTheDecision	The restraints provided by the Constitution on the legislative powers or the execut ive authority of the <ORG> thus operate as guarantees of life and personal liberty of the individu- als.
RatioOfTheDecision	Deprivation (total loss) of personal liberty, which inter alia includes the right to eat or sleep when one likes or to work or not to work as and when one pleases and sever- al such rights sought to be protected by the expression "personal liberty" in article 21 , is quite different from restriction (which is only a partial control) of the right to move freely (which is relatively a minor right of a citizen) as safeguarded by article 19 (1) (d).
RatioOfTheDecision	Deprivation of personal liberty has not the same meaning as restriction of free movement in the territory of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is made clear when the provisions of the Criminal Procedure Code in Chapter VIII relating to security of peace or maintenance of public order are read.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore article 19 (5) cannot apply to a substantive law depriving a citizen of personal liberty.
RatioOfTheDecision	I am unable to accept the contention that the word "deprivation" includes within its scope "res triction" when interpreting article 21.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 22 envisages the law of preventive detention.
RatioOfTheDecision	So does article 9.46 read with Schedule Seven, List I, Entry 9, and List III, Entry 3.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, when the subject of preventive detention is specifically dealt with in the Chapter on Fundamental Rights I do not think it is proper to consider a legisla- tion ' permitting preventive detention as in conflict with the rights mentioned in article 19 (1).
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 19 (1) does not purport to cover all aspects of liberty or of personal liberty.
RatioOfTheDecision	In that article only certain phases of liberty are dealt with.
RatioOfTheDecision	"Personal liberty" would primarily mean liberty of the physical body.
RatioOfTheDecision	The rights given under article 19 (1) do not directly come under that description.
RatioOfTheDecision	They are rights which accompany the freedom or liberty of the person.
RatioOfTheDecision	By their very nature they are freedoms of a person assumed to be in full possession of his personal liberty.
Precedent	If article 19 is considered to be the only article safeguarding personal liberty several wel l-recognised rights, as for instance, the right to eat or drink, the right to work, play, swim and numerous other rights and activities and even the right to life will not be deemed protected under the Constitution.
Precedent	I do not think that is the intention.
Precedent	It seems to me improper to read article 19 as dealing with the same subject as article 21.
Precedent	Article 19 gives the rights specified therein only to the citizens of India while arti- cle 21 is applicable to all persons.
Precedent	The word citizen is expressly defined in the Constitution to indicate only a certain section of the inhabitants of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	Moreover, the protection given by article 21 is very general.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is of "law"--whatever that expression is interpreted to mean.
RatioOfTheDecision	The legislative restrictions on the law-making powers of the legislature are not here prescribed in detail as in the case of the rights specified in article 19.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion there- fore article should be read as a separate complete article.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 21 which is also in Part III under the caption "Right  to Freedom" runs as follows :-  "No person shall be deprived of his life or personal liberty except according to procedure established by law."
RatioOfTheDecision	This article has been strongly relied upon by the peti- tioner in support of his contention that the impugned Act is ultra vires the <ORG> as it abridges the right given by this article to every person.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was argued that under the Constitution of the United States of America the corre- sponding provision is found in the 5th and 14th Amendments where the provision, inter alia, is "that no person shall be deprived of his life or liberty or property except by due process of law."
RatioOfTheDecision	It was contended for the petitioner that the Indian Constitution gives the same protection to every person in India, except that in the 'United States "due process of law" has been .construed by its <ORG> to cover both substantive and procedural law, while in India only the protection of procedural law is guaranteed.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was contend- ed that the omission of the word "due" made no d ifference to the interpretation of the words in article 21.
RatioOfTheDecision	The word "established"' was not equivalent to "prescribed".
RatioOfTheDecision	It had a wider meaning.
RatioOfTheDecision	The word "law" did not mean enacted law because that will be no 'legislative protection at all.
RatioOfTheDecision	If so construed, any Act passed by the <ORG> or <ORG>, which was otherwise within its legislative power, can destroy or abridge this right.
RatioOfTheDecision	On the same line of reasoning, it was argued that if that was the inten- tion there was no necessity to put this as a fundamental right in Part III at all.
RatioOfTheDecision	As to the meaning of the word "law" it was argued that it meant principles of natural justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	It meant "jus", i.e., law in the abstract sense of the principles of natural justice, as mentioned in standard works of Jurisprudence, and not "lex", i.e., enact- ed law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Against the contention that such construction will leave the meaning vague, it was argued that four principles of natural justice recognised in all civilized countries were covered, in any event, by th e word "law".
RatioOfTheDecision	They are: (1) An objective test, i.e., a certain, definite and ascer- tainable rule of human conduct for the violation of which one can be detained; (2) Notice of the grounds of such detention; (3) An impartial tribunal, administrative, judi- cial or advisory, to decide whether the detention is justi- fied; and (4) Orderly course of procedure, including an opportunity to be heard orally (not merely by making a written representation) with a right to lead evidence and call witnesses.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion, this line of approach is not proper and indeed is misleading.
RatioOfTheDecision	As regards the American Constitution its general structure is noticed in these words in "The Government of the United States" by <NAME> (5th Edition) at page 53: "The architects of 1787 built only the basement.
RatioOfTheDecision	Their descendants have kept adding walls and windows, wings and gables, pillars and porches to make a rambling structure which is not yet finished.
RatioOfTheDecision	Or, to change the metaphor, it has a fabric which, to use the words of <NAME> <NAME>, is still being 'woven on the roaring loom of time'.
RatioOfTheDecision	That is what the framers of the original Constitution intended it to be.
RatioOfTheDecision	Never was it in their mind to work out a final scheme for the government of the country and stereotype it for all time.
RatioOfTheDecision	They sought merely to pro- vide a starting point."
RatioOfTheDecision	The same aspect is emphasized in Professor <NAME>'s book on Constitutional Law and Cooley's Constitutional Limitations.
RatioOfTheDecision	In contrast to the American Constitution, the Indian Constitution is a very detailed one.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution itself provides in minute details the legislative powers of the <ORG> and <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The same feature is noticeable in the case of the judiciary, finance, trade, commerce and services.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is thus quite detailed and the whole of it has to be read with the same sanctity, without giving undue weight to Part III or article 246 , except to the extent one is legitimately and clearly limited by the other.
RatioOfTheDecision	Four marked points of distinction between the clause in the American Co nstitution and article 21 of the Constitution of India may be noticed at this stage.
RatioOfTheDecision	The first is that in U.S A.
RatioOfTheDecision	Constitution the word "liberty" is used simpliciter while in India it is restricted to personal liberty.
RatioOfTheDecision	(2) In U.S.A. Constitution the same protection is given to proper- ty, while in India the fundamental right in respect of property is contained in article 31.
RatioOfTheDecision	(3) The word "due" is omitted altogether and the expression "due process of law" is not used deliberately.
RatioOfTheDecision	(4) The word "established" is used and is limited to "Procedure" in Our article 21.
RatioOfTheDecision	The whole argument of the petitioner is rounded on the meaning of the word "law" given to it by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It seems unnecessary to embark on a discussion of the powers and jurisdiction of <ORG> and how they came to enlarge or abridge the meaning of law in the expression "due process of law".
RatioOfTheDecision	Without going into details, I think there is no justification to adopt the meaning of the word "law" as interpreted b y <ORG> in the expression "due process of law" merely because the word "law" is used in article 21.
RatioOfTheDecision	The discussion of the meaning of "due process of law" found in <NAME> on Constitutional Law and in <NAME> Constitutional Limitations shows the diverse meanings given to that expression at different times and under dif- ferent circumstances by <ORG>, so much so that the conclusion reached by these authors is that the expression.
RatioOfTheDecision	means reasonable law according to the view of the majority of the judges of <ORG> at a particular time holding office.
RatioOfTheDecision	It also shows how the meaning of the expression was widened or abridged in certain decades.
RatioOfTheDecision	Moreover, to control the meaning so given to that expression from time to time the doctrine of police powers was brought into play.
RatioOfTheDecision	That doctrine, shortly put, is that legislation meant for the good of the people generally, and in which the individual has to surrender his freedom to a certain extent because it is for the  benefit of the people at large, has not to be tested by the touchstone of the "due process of law" formula.
RatioOfTheDecision	Our attention was drawn to the debates and report of the drafting committee of <ORG> in respect of the wording of this clause.
RatioOfTheDecision	The report may be read not to control the meaning of the article, but may be seen in case of ambiguity.
Precedent	In <ORG> v. The Commonwealth(1), it was thought that individu- al opinion of members of the <ORG> expressed in the debate cannot be referred to for the purpose of construing the Constitution.
Precedent	The same opinion was expressed in United States v. <ORG>).
Precedent	The result appears to be that while it is not proper to take into consideration the indi- vidual opinions of Members of <ORG> or <ORG> to construe the meaning of the particular clause, when a ques- tion is raised whether a certain phrase or expression was up for consideration at all or not, a reference to the debates may be' permitted.
Precedent	In the present case the de bates were referred to to show that the expression "due process of law" was known to exist in the American Constitution (1) (1904) 1 Com.
Precedent	L.R.
Precedent	208.
Precedent	(2) (169) U.S 649 at 699.
Precedent	111  and after a discussion was not adopted by <ORG> in our Constitution.
Precedent	<CITATION_427436> (1), a reference to the proceedings of the <ORG> which resulted in the passing of the Act was not considered legitimate aid in the construction of a particular section.
Precedent	The same reasons were held as cogent for excluding a reference to such debates in construing an Indian Statute.
Precedent	Resort may be had to these sources with- great caution and only when latent ambiguities are to be resolved.
Precedent	See <NAME>' Statute Law (4th Edition) page 122, <ORG> (9th Edition)pp.
Precedent	28- 29 and <ORG> (1940 Edition) p. 379, article 214.
Precedent	A perusal of the report of the drafting committee to which our attention was drawn shows clearly that <ORG> had before it the American arti cle and the expression "due process of law" but they deliberately dropped the use of that expression from our Constitution.
Precedent	No extrinsic aid is needed to interpret the words of article 21 , which in my opinion, are not ambiguous.Normally read, and without thinking of other Constitutions, the expression "procedure established by law" must mean procedure prescribed by the law of the <ORG>.
Precedent	If the Indian Constitution wanted to preserve to every person the protection given by the due process clause of the Ameri- can Constitution there was nothing to prevent the <ORG> from adopting the phrase, or if they wanted to limit the same to procedure only, to adopt that expression with only the word "procedural" prefixed to "law."
Precedent	However, the correct question is what is the right given by article 21 ?
Precedent	The only right is that no person shall be deprived of his life or liberty except according to procedure established by law.
Precedent	One may like that right to cover a larger area, but to give such a right is not the function of  the <ORG>; it is the function of the Constitution.
Precedent	To read the word "law" as meaning rules of natural justice will land one in (1) (1895)L.R.
Precedent	221.
Precedent	A.
Precedent	107.
Precedent	15  difficulties because the rules of natural justice, as re- gards procedure, are nowhere defined and in my opinion the Constitution cannot be read as laying down a vague standard.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is particularly so when in omitting to adopt "due process of law" it was considered that the expression "procedure established by law" made the standard specific.
RatioOfTheDecision	It can not be specific except by reading the expression as meaning procedure prescribed by the legislature.
RatioOfTheDecision	The word "law" as used in this Part has different shades of meaning but in no other article it appears to bear the indefinite meaning of natural justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	If so, there appears no reason why in this article it should receive this peculiar meaning.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 31 which is also in Part III and relates to the fundamental rights in respect of property runs as follows :-  "No person shall be deprived of his property sa ve by authority of law."
RatioOfTheDecision	It is obvious that in that clause "law" must mean enact- ed law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The object of dealing with property under a differ- ent article appears more to provide the exceptions found in article 31 (2) to (6), rather than to give the word "law" a different meaning than the one given in article 21.
RatioOfTheDecision	The world "established" according to <ORG> means "to fix, settle, institute or ordain by enactment or agree- ment."
RatioOfTheDecision	The word "established" itself suggests an agency which fixes the limits.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to the dictionary this agency can be either the legislature or an agreement between the parties.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is therefore no justification to give the meaning of "jus" to "law" in article 21.
RatioOfTheDecision	The phrase "procedure established by law" seems to be borrowed from article 31 of the Japanese Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	But other articles of that Constitution which expressly pre- serve other personal liberties in different clauses have to be read together to determine the meaning of "law" in the expression "proced ure established by law."
Precedent	These articles of the Japanese Constitution have not been incorporated in the Constitution of India in the same language.
Precedent	It is not shown that the word "law" means "jus" in the Japanese Constitution.
Precedent	In the Japanese Constitution these rights claimed under the rules of natural justice are not given by the interpretation of the words "procedure established by law" in their article 31.
Precedent	The word "due" in the expression "due process of law" in the American Consti- tution is interpreted to mean "just," according to the opinion of <ORG> That word imparts jurisdiction to the <ORG> to pronounce what is "due" from otherwise, according to law.
Precedent	The deliberate omission of the word "due" from article 21 lends strength to the conten- tion that the justiciable aspect of "law", i.e., to consider whether it is reasonable or not by the <ORG>, does not form part of the Indian Constitution.
Precedent	The omission of the word "due", the limitation imposed by the word "procedure" and the insert ion of the word "established" thus brings out more clearly the idea of legislative prescription in the expres- sion used in article 21.
Precedent	By adopting the phrase "procedure established by law" the Constitution gave the legislature the final word to determine the law.
Precedent	Our attention was drawn to The King v. The Military Governor of the Hair Park Camp ('), where articles 6 and 70 of the Irish Constitution are discussed.
Precedent	Under article 6 it is provided that the liberty of the person is inviolable and no person shall be deprived of such except "in accord- ance with law" ......In article 70 it is provided that no one shall be tried "save in due course of law" and extraor- dinary Courts were not permitted to be established except <ORG> to try military offences.
Precedent	The expres- sion "in accordance with law" was interpreted to mean not rules of natural justice but as the law in force at the time.
Precedent	<ORG> gave the expression "due course of law" the meaning given to it according to the English law and not  the American law.
Precedent	It was observed by Lord <NAME> in <NAME> v. Officer Administering the Government of Nigeria C), that in accordance with British Jurispru- dence no member of the executive can interfere with the liberty or property of a British subject except when he can support the legality of his act before <ORG>.

RatioOfTheDecision	It seems very arguable that in the whole set-up of Part III of our Constitution these principles only remain guaranteed by article 21.
RatioOfTheDecision	A detailed discussion of the true limits of article 21 will not be necessary if article 22 is considered a code to the extent there are provisions therein for preventive detention.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this.
RatioOfTheDecision	connection it may be noticed that the articles in Part III deal with different and separate rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under the caption  "Right to Freedom" articles 19--22 are grouped but each with a separate marginal note.

RatioOfTheDecision	If the procedure mentioned in those articles is followed the arrest and detention contemplated by article 22 (1) and (2 ), although they infringe the personal liberty of the individual, will be legal, because that becomes the established legal proce- dure in respect of arrest and detention.
Precedent	Article 22 is for protection against arrest and detention in certain cases, and runs as follows :--  "22.

RatioOfTheDecision	The learned Attorney-General contended that the subject of preventive detention does not fall under  article 21 at all and is covered wholly by article 22.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to him, article 22 is a complete code.
RatioOfTheDecision	I am unable to accept that contention.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is obvious that in respect of arrest and detention article 22 (1) and (2) provide safeguards.
RatioOfTheDecision	These safeguards are excluded in the case of preventive detention by article 22 (3 ), but safe- guards in connection with such detention are provided by clauses (4) to (7) of the same article.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is therefore clear that article 21 has to be read as supplemented by article 22.
RatioOfTheDecision	Reading in that way the proper mode of construc- tion will be that to the extent the procedure is prescribed by article 22 the same is to be observed; otherwise art icle 21 will apply.
RatioOfTheDecision	But if certain procedural safeguards are expressly stated as not required, or specific rules on certain points of procedure are prescribed, it seems im- proper to interpret these points as not covered by article 22 and left open for consideration under article 21.
RatioOfTheDecision	To the extent the points are dealt with, and included or excluded,, article 22 is a complete code.
RatioOfTheDecision	On the points of procedure which expressly or by necessary implication are not dealt with by article 22 , the operation of article 21 will remain unaffected.
Precedent	It is thus necessary first to look at article 22 (4) to (7) and next at the provisions 0 the impugned Act to determine if the Act or any of its provi- sions are ultra vires.
Precedent	It may be noticed that neither the American nor the Japanese Constitution contain provisions permitting preventive detention,.
Precedent	much less laying down limitations on such right of detention, in normal timeS, i.e., without a declaration of emergency.
Precedent	Preventive deten- tion in normal times,.
Precedent	i.e., without the e xistence of an emergency like war,.

RatioOfTheDecision	The provisions of article 22 (4) to (7) by their very wording leave unaffected the large powers of legislation on this point and emphasize particularly by article 22 (7) the power of the <ORG> to deprive a person of a right to have his case considered by an advisory board.
RatioOfTheDecision	Part III and.
RatioOfTheDecision	117  article 22 in particular are the only restrictions on that power and but for those provisions the power to legislate on this subject would have been quite unrestricted.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> could have made a law without any safeguard or any procedure for preventive detention.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such an autocratic supremacy of the legislature is certainly cut down by article 21.
RatioOfTheDecision	There- fore, if the legislature prescribes a procedure by a validly enacted law and such proc edure in the case of preventive detention does not come in conflict with the express provi- sions of Part III or article 22 (4) to (7), the Preventive Detention Act must be held valid notwithstanding that the Court may not fully approve of the procedure prescribed under such Act.
Statute	Article 22 (4) opens with a double negative.
Statute	Put in a positive form it will mean that a law which provides for preventive detention for a period longer than three months shall contain .a provision establishing an advisory board, (consisting of persons with the qualifications mentioned in sub-clause (a)), and which has to report before the expira- tion of three months if in its opinion there was sufficient cause for such detention.
Statute	This clause, if it stood by itself and without the remaining provisions of article 22 , will apply both to the <ORG> and <ORG>.
Statute	The proviso to this clause further enjoins that even though the advisory board may be of the opinion that there was sufficient cause for such detention, i.e ., detention beyond the period of three months, still the detention is not to be permitted beyond the maximum period, if any, prescribed by <ORG> under article 22 (7) (b).
RatioOfTheDecision	Again the whole of this sub-clause is made inoperative by article 22 (4) (b) in respect of an Act of preventive detention passed by <ORG> under clauses (7) (a) .and (b).
RatioOfTheDecision	Inasmuch as the im- pugned Act is an Act of the <ORG> purported to be so made, clause 22 (4) has no operation and may for the present discussion be kept aside.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 22 prescribes that when any person under a preventive detention law is detained, the authority making the order shall, as soon as may be, commu- nicate to such person the grounds on which .
RatioOfTheDecision	the order has been made and shall afford him the earliest opportunity of making a representation against the order.
RatioOfTheDecision	This clause is of general operation in respect of every detention order made under any law permitting deten- tion.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 22 (6) permits the authority making the order to withhold disclosure  of facts which such authority consid- ers against the public interest to disclose.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be noticed that this clause only permits the non-disclosure of facts, and reading clauses (5) and (6) together a distinc- tion is drawn between facts and grounds of detention.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 22 (4) and (7) deal not with the period of detention only but with other requirements in the case of preventive detention also.
RatioOfTheDecision	They provide for the establishment of an advisory board, and the necessity of furnishing grounds to the detenue and also to give him a right to make a represen- tation.
RatioOfTheDecision	Reading article 22 clauses (4) and (7) together it appears to be implied that preventive detention for less than three months, without an advisory board, is permitted under the Chapter on Fundamental Rights, provided such legislation is within the legislative competence of the <ORG> or <ORG>, as the case may be.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 22 (5) permits the detained person to make a representation.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution is silent as to the person to w hom it has to be made, or how it has to be dealt with.
RatioOfTheDecision	But that is the procedure laid down by the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It does not therefore mean that if a law made by the <ORG> in respect of preventive detention does not make provision on those two points it is invalid.
RatioOfTheDecision	Silence on these points does not make the impugned Act in contravention of the Constitution because the first question is what are the rights given by the Constitution in the case of preventive detention.
RatioOfTheDecision	The contention that the representation should be to an outside body has no support in law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even in the <NAME> case the representation had to be made to the Secretary of <ORG> and not to another body.
RatioOfTheDecision	After such representation was made, another advisory board had to consider it, but it was not necessary to make the represen- tation itself to a third party.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 22 (4) and (7) permit the non-establishment of an advisory board expressly in a parliamentary legislation-  119  providing for preventive detention beyond three months.
RatioOfTheDecision	If so, how  can it be urged that the nonestablishment of an advisory.
RatioOfTheDecision	board is a fundamental right violated by the procedure prescribed in the Act passed by the <ORG>?
RatioOfTheDecision	The important clause to be considered is article 22 (7).
RatioOfTheDecision	Sub-clause (a) is important-for this case.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the case of an Act of preventive detention passed by the <ORG> this clause contained in the Chapter on Fundamental Rights, thus permits detention beyond a period of three months and ex- cludes the necessity of consulting an advisory board, if the opening words of the sub-clause are complied with.
RatioOfTheDecision	Sub- clause (b) is permissive.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not obligatory on the <ORG> to prescribe any maximum period.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was argued that this gives the <ORG> a right to allow a person to be detained indefinitely.
RatioOfTheDecision	If that construction is correct, it springs out of the words of sub-clause (7) itself and the <ORG> cannot help in the matter.
RatioOfTheDecision	Subclause (c) permits the <ORG> to lay down the procedure to be followed by the advisory board in an inquiry under sub-c lause (a) of clause (4).
RatioOfTheDecision	I am unable to accept the contention that article 22 (4) (a) is the rule and article 22 (7) the exception.
RatioOfTheDecision	I read them as two alternatives provided by the Constitution for making laws on preventive detention.
RatioOfTheDecision	Bearing in mind the provisions of article 22 read with article 246 and Schedule VII, List I, Entry 9, and List III, Entry 3, it is thus clear that the <ORG> is empowered to enact a law of preventive detention (a) for reasons connected with defence, (b) for reasons connected with foreign affairs, (c) for reasons connected with the security of India; and (under List III), (d) for reasons connected with the security of a <ORG>, (e) for reasons connected with the maintenance of public order, or (f) for reasons connect- ed with the maintenance of supplies and services essential to the community.

Precedent	It was next contended that by section 12 the <ORG> had provided that a person might be detained for a period longer than three months but not exceeding one year from the date of his detention, without obtaining the opinion of an advisory board, with a view to prevent him from acting in any manner prejudicial to (a) the defence of India, rela- tions of India with foreign powers or the security of India; or (b)the security of a <ORG> or the maintenance of public order.
