RatioOfTheDecision	In the second place, it appears to me that when the same words are not used, it will be against the ordinary canons of con- struction to interpret a provision in our Constitution in accordance with the interpretation put upon a so mewhat analogous provision in the Constitution of another country, where not only the language is different, but the entire political conditions and constitutional set-up are dissimi- lar.
RatioOfTheDecision	In <ORG>, stress has been laid uniformly upon the word "due" which occurs before and quali- fies the expression "process of law."
RatioOfTheDecision	"Due" means " what is just and proper" according to the circumstances of a particular case.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is this word which introduces the varia- ble element in the application of the doctrine; for what is reasonable in one set of circumstances may not be so in another and a different set.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the Indian Constitution the word "due" has been deliberately omitted and this shows clearly that the Constitution-makers of India had no inten- tion of introducing the American doctrine.
RatioOfTheDecision	The word "estab- lished" ordinarily means "fixed or laid down" and if "law" means, as Mr. <NAME> contends, not any particular piece of law but the indefinite and indefinable principles of natural justice which  underlie positive systems of law, it would not at all be appropriate to use the expression "established," for natural law or natural justice cannot establish anything like a definite procedure.
RatioOfTheDecision	It does not appear that in any part of the Constitution the word "law" has been used in the sense of "general law" connoting what has been described as the principles of natural justice outside the realm of positive law.

RatioOfTheDecision	By "classes of cases" we mean certain determinable groups, the individu als comprised in each group being related to one another in a particular way which constitutes the determining factor of that group.
RatioOfTheDecision	"Circumstances" on the other hand connote situations or conditions which are external to the persons concerned.
RatioOfTheDecision	Preventive detention can be provided for by law for reasons connected with six different ,matters specified in the relevant items in the legislative lists, and whatever the reasons might be, there is a provision contained in article 22 (4) (a) which lays down that deten- tion for more than three months could not be permitted except with the sanction of the advisory board.
RatioOfTheDecision	An alterna- tive however has been provided for by clause (b) and Parlia- ment has been given the option to take away the protection given by clause (a) and specify the circumstances and the cases when this rule will not apply.

RatioOfTheDecision	The basis  of classification has been the apprehended acts of the persons detained described with reference to the general heads mentioned in the items in the legislative lists as said above.
RatioOfTheDecision	Five out of the six heads have been taken out and labelled as classes of cases to which the protection of clause (4) (a) of the article would not be available.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is against common sense that all forms of activities connected with these five items are equally dangerous and merit the same drastic treatment.
RatioOfTheDecision	The descriptions are very general and there may be acts of various degrees of intensity and danger under each one of these heads.
RatioOfTheDecision	Although I do not think that section 12 has been framed with due regard to the object which the Constitution had in view, I am unable to say that the section is invalid as being ultra vires the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution has given unfettered powers to <ORG> in the matter of making the classifications and it is open to the <ORG> to adopt any method or principle as it likes.
RatioOfTheDecision	If it chose t he principle implied in the enumeration of subjects under the relevant legislative heads, it cannot be said that <ORG> has exceeded its powers.
RatioOfTheDecision	I am also unable to hold that both "circumstances" as well as "classes" have to be prescribed in order to comply with the requirement of sub-clause (a) of article 22 (7).
RatioOfTheDecision	The sub-clause (a) of the article lays down a purely enabling provision and <ORG>, if it so chooses, may pass any legislation in terms of the same.
RatioOfTheDecision	Where an optional power is conferred on certain authority to perform two separate acts, ordinarily it would not be obligatory upon it to perform both; it may do either if it so likes.
RatioOfTheDecision	Here the classes have been specified and the classes apparently are composed of persons who are detained for the purpose of preventing them from committing certain apprehended acts.
RatioOfTheDecision	I am extremely doubtful whether the classes themselves could be described as "circumstances" as they purport to have been done in the section.
RatioOfTheDecision	"Circumstances" would ordinarily refer to  conditions like war, rebellion, communal disturbances and things like that, under which extra precaution might be :necessary and the detention of suspected persons beyond the period of three months without the sanction of the advisory board might be justified.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is said that the likelihood of these persons committing the particular acts which are specified might constitute "circumstances."
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion, that is not a plain and sensible interpretation.
RatioOfTheDecision	But whatev- er that may be, as I am of opinion that it is not obligatory on <ORG> to prescribe both the circumstances and the classes of cases, I am unable to hold that section 12 is ultra vires the Constitution because the circumstances are not mentioned.
RatioOfTheDecision	As I have said at the beginning, the draft is rather clumsy and I do not know why <ORG> used the word "or" when in the Constitution itself the word "and" has been used.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the fourth and last point raised by Mr. <NAME> the principal question for consideration is the validity of section 14 of the  Preventive Detention Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	Subsection (1)of section 14 prohibits any <ORG> from allowing any statement to be made or any evidence to be given before it of the substance of any communication made under section 7 of the grounds on which a detention order has been made against any person or any representation made by him against such order.
Statute	It further provides that no <ORG> shall be entitled to require any public officer to produce before it or to disclose the substance of any such communication or representation made or the proceedings of an advisory board or that part of the report of an advisory board which is confidential.
Statute	Sub-section (2) further provides that:  "It shall be an offence punishable with imprisonment for a term which may extend to one year, or with fine, or with both, for any person to disclose or publish without the previous authorisation of <ORG> or <ORG>, as the' case may be, any contents or matter purporting to be contents of any such communication or represe ntation as is referred to in sub-section (1):  Provided that nothing in this sub-section shall apply to a disclosure made to his legal adviser by a person who is the subject of a detention order."
RatioOfTheDecision	The provisions of this section are obviously of a most drastic character.
RatioOfTheDecision	It imposes a ban on the <ORG> and pre- vents it from allowing any statement to be made or any evidence produced before it of the substance of any communi- cation made to the detenu apprising him of the grounds upon which the detention order was made.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> is also incompetent to look into the proceedings before the advisory board or the report of the latter which is confidential.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further the disclosure of such materials has been made a criminal offence punishable with imprisonment for a term which may extend to one year.
RatioOfTheDecision	Mr. <NAME>'s contention is that these restrictions render utterly nugatory the provi- sions of article 32 of the Constitution which guarantees to every person the right to move this <ORG> by appropriate proceedings for t he enforcement of the rights conferred by Part III of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not disputed that the petitioner has the right of moving this Court for a writ of habeas corpus, and unless the Court is in a position to look into and examine the grounds upon which the detention order has been made, it is impossible for it to come to any deci- sion on the point and pass a proper judgment.
RatioOfTheDecision	Though the right to move this Court is not formally taken away, the entire proceedings are rendered ineffective and altogether illusory.
RatioOfTheDecision	On behalf of the respondent, it is pointed out that article 32 guarantees only the right to constitutional remedy for enforcement of the rights which are declared by the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	If there are no rights under the Constitution, guaranteed to a person who is detained under any law of preventive deten- tion, no question of enforcing such rights by an ap- proach to this Court at all arises.
RatioOfTheDecision	I do not think that this argument proceeds on a sound basis; and in my opinion, section 14 does take away  and materially curtails some of the fundamental rights which are guaranteed by the Constitu- tion itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 22 , clause (5), of the Constitution lays down as a fundamental right that when a person is detained for preventive detention, the authority making the order shall, as soon as may be, communicate to such person the grounds on which the order has been made, and shall afford him the earliest opportunity of making a representa- tion against the order.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under clause (6), the authority need not disclose such facts as it considers to be against public interest to disclose.
RatioOfTheDecision	But so far as the grounds are concerned, the disclosure is not prohibited under any cir- cumstance.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also incumbent upon the detaining authori- ty to afford a detenu the earliest opportunity of making a representation against the detention order.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has been held in several cases, and in my opinion quite rightly, that if the grounds supplied to a detained person are of such a vague and indefinite character that no proper and adequ ate representation could be made in reply to the same, that itself would be an infraction of the right which has been given to the detenu under law.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion, it would not be possible for the <ORG> to decide whether the provisions of article 22 , clause (5), have been duly complied with and the fundamental right guaranteed by it has been made avail- able to the detenu unless the grounds communicated to him under the provisions of this article are actually produced before the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Apart from this, it is also open to.
RatioOfTheDecision	the person detained to contend that the detention order has been a main fide exercise of power by the detain- ing authority and that the grounds upon which it is based, are not proper or relevant grounds which would justify detention under the-provisions of the law itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	These rights of the detenu would for all practical purposes be rendered unenforceable if the <ORG> is precluded from look- ing into the grounds which have been supplied to him under section 7 of the Preventive Detention A ct.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion, section 14 of the Preventive Detention Act does materially affect the fundamental rights declared under Part III of the Constitution and for this reason it must be held to be illegal and ultra vires.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not disputed, however, that this section can be severed from the rest of the Act without affecting the other provisions of the Act in any way.
RatioOfTheDecision	The whole Act cannot, therefore, be held to be ultra vires.
RatioOfTheDecision	Mr. <NAME> has further argued that section 3 of the Act also contravenes the provisions of article 32 of the Consti- tution, for it makes satisfaction of the particular authori- ties final in matters of preventive detention and thereby prevents this <ORG> from satisfying itself as to the propriety of the detention order.
RatioOfTheDecision	This contention cannot succeed as no infraction of any fundamental right is in- volved in it.
RatioOfTheDecision	As has been pointed out already, this <ORG> cannot interfere unless it is proved that the power has been exercised by the authorities in a <NAME> fide manner or that the grounds ar e not proper or relevant grounds which justify detention.
RatioOfTheDecision	The provisions are undoubtedly harsh, but as they do not take away the rights under articles 21 and 22 of the Constitution, they cannot be held to be illegal or ultra vires.
RatioOfTheDecision	The result, therefore, is that, in my opinion, the Preventive Detention Act must be declared to be intra vires the Constitution with the exception of section 14 which is held to be illegal and ultra vires.
Argument	The present petition, however, must stand dismissed, though it may be open to the petitioner to make a fresh application if he so chooses and if the grounds that have been supplied to him under section 7 of the Act do furnish adequate reasons for making such application.
Argument	286  DAS J.--I am likewise of opinion that this application should be dismissed.
Argument	The contention of learned counsel appearing in support of this application is that the provisions of the Preventive Detention Act, 1950 (Act IV of 1950), are extremely drastic and wholly unreasonable and take away or, in any event , considerably abridge the fundamental rights conferred on the citizens by the provisions of Part III of the Constitution and that this <ORG> should declare the Act wholly void under article 13 (2) of the Constitution and set the petitioner at liberty.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is necessary to bear in mind the scope and ambit of the powers of the <ORG> under the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The powers of the <ORG> are not the same under all Constitutions.
RatioOfTheDecision	In England <ORG> is supreme and there is no limitation upon its legislative powers.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, a law duly made by <ORG> cannot be challenged in any <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The English <ORG>s have to interpret and apply the law; they have no authority to declare such a law illegal or unconstitutional.
RatioOfTheDecision	By the American Constitution the' legislative power of the Union is vested in the <ORG> and in a sense the <ORG> is the supreme legislative power.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the written Constitu- tion of the United States is supreme above all the three limbs of Government and, therefore, the law made by the <ORG>, in o rder to be valid, must be in conformity with the provisions of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	If it is not, <ORG> will intervene and declare that law to be unconstitutional and void.
RatioOfTheDecision	As will be seen more fully hereafter, <ORG> of the United States, under the leadership of Chief Justice <NAME>, assumed the power to.
RatioOfTheDecision	declare any law unconstitutional on the ground of its not being in "due process of law," an expression to be found in the Fifth Amendment (1791) of the United States Constitution and the Fourteenth Amendment (1868) which related to the State Constitutions.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is thus that <ORG> established its own supremacy over the executive and the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	In India the position of the <ORG> is some- where in between the Courts in England and the United States.
RatioOfTheDecision	While in the main leaving our <ORG> and the State Legisla- tures supreme in their respective legislative fields, our Constitution has, by some of the articles, put upon the <ORG> certain specified limitations some  of which will have to be discussed hereafter.
RatioOfTheDecision	The point to be noted, however, is that in so far as there is any limitation on the legislative power, the <ORG> must, on a complaint being made to it, scrutinise and ascertain whether such limitation has been transgressed and if there has been any transgression the <ORG> will courageously declare the law unconstitution- al, for the <ORG> is bound by its oath to uphold the Consti- tution.
RatioOfTheDecision	But outside the limitations imposed on the legisla- tive powers our <ORG> and the State Legislatures are supreme in their respective legislative fields and the <ORG> has no authority to question the wisdom or policy of the law duly made by the appropriate legislature.
RatioOfTheDecision	Our Constitution, unlike the English Constitution, recognises the <ORG>'s supremacy over the legislative authority, but such supremacy is a very limited one, for it is confined to the field where the legislative power is circumscribed by limitations put upon it by the Constitution itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	Within this restrict-  ed field the <ORG> may, on a scrutiny of the law made by the <ORG>, declare it void if it is found to have trans- gressed the constitutional limitations.
RatioOfTheDecision	But our Constitu- tion, unlike the American Constitution, does not recognise the absolute supremacy of the <ORG> over the legislative authority in all respects, for outside the restricted field of constitutional limitations our <ORG> and the State <ORG>s are supreme in their respective legislative fields and in that wider field there is no scope for the <ORG> in India to play the role of the Supreme <ORG> of the United States.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is well for us to constantly remember this basic limitation on our own powers.
RatioOfTheDecision	The impugned Act has been passed by <ORG> after the Constitution came into force.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 246 gives exclusive power to <ORG> to make laws with respect to any of the matters enumerated in List I in the Seventh Schedule and it gives exclusive power to the State <ORG>s to make laws with respect to any of the matters specifie d in List II of that Schedule.
RatioOfTheDecision	It also gives concurrent power to <ORG> as well as to <ORG> to make laws with respect to any of the matters enumerated in List III in the Seventh Schedule.
RatioOfTheDecision	Residuary powers of legislation are vested in parliament under article  248.
RatioOfTheDecision	The first thing to note is that under Entry 9 of List I the parliament and under Entry 3 in List III both parliament and <ORG> are empowered to make laws for preventive detention for reasons connected with the several matters specified in the respective entries.
RatioOfTheDecision	This legisla- tion is not conditioned upon the existence of any war with a foreign power or upon the proclamation of emergency under Part XVIII of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Our Constitution has, there- fore, accepted preventive detention as the subjectmatter of peacetime legislation as distinct from emergency legisla- tion.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is a novel feature to provide for preventive detention in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no such provision in the Constitution of any other c ountry that I know of.
RatioOfTheDecision	Be that as it may, for reasons good or bad, our Constitution has deliberately and plainly given power to <ORG> and <ORG> to enact preventive detention laws even in peacetime.
RatioOfTheDecision	To many of us a preventive detention law is odious at all times but what I desire to emphasise is that it is not for the <ORG> to question the wisdom and policy of the Constitution which the people have given unto themselves.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is another basic fact which the <ORG> must not overlook.
RatioOfTheDecision	The next thing to bear in mind is that, if there were nothing else in the Constitution, the legislative powers of <ORG> and <ORG> in their respective fields would have been absolute.
RatioOfTheDecision	In such circumstances the <ORG> would have been entitled only to scrutinise whether <ORG> or <ORG> had, in making a partic- ular law, over-.
RatioOfTheDecision	stepped its legislative field and en- croached upon the legislative field of the other legislative power, but could not have otherwise questioned th e validity of any law made by the parliament or the State <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	289  Thus under Entry 9 of List I the <ORG> and under Entry 3 of List III the <ORG> and <ORG> could make as drastic a preventive detention law as it pleased.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such a law might have authorised a policeman, not to speak of a District Magistrate or Sub-Divisional Magistrate or the Commissioner of Police, to take a man, citizen or non-citi- zen, into custody and keep him in detention for as long as he pleased.
RatioOfTheDecision	This law might not have made any provision for supplying to the detenu the grounds of his detention or affording any opportunity to him to make any representation to anybody or for setting up any advisory board at all.
RatioOfTheDecision	Likewise, under Entries 1 and 2 in List III the <ORG> or <ORG> might have added as many new and novel offences as its fancy might have dictated and provided for any cruel penalty ranging from the maiming of the limbs to boiling to death in oil or repealed the whole of the Cod e of Criminal Procedure and provided for trial by battle or ordeal or for conviction by the verdict of a sorcerer or a soothsayer.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such law might have forbidden any speech criti- cising the Government, however mildly, or banned all public meetings or prohibited formation of all associations under penalty of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under Entry 33 of List I the <ORG> might have made a law for acquiring anybody's properties for the purposes of the <ORG> without any compensation and under Entry 36 in List III <ORG> could do the same subject to the provisions of Entry 42 in List III which empowers the making of a law laying down principles for payment of compensation which might be anything above noth- ing.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under Entry 81 <ORG> could have made any law restricting or even prohibiting inter-State migration so that a Bengali would not be able to move into and settle in Bihar or vice versa.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is needless to multiply instances of atrocious laws which <ORG> or <ORG> might have made under art icle 246 read with the different lists if there were nothing else in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Our Legislatures, subject to the limitation of distribution of legislative powers, would have been as supreme in their respective legislative fields as the English <ORG> is and has been.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> in India, in such event, would have had to take the law duly made, inter- pret it and apply it.
RatioOfTheDecision	It would not have been entitled to utter a word as to the propriety of the particular law, although it might have shuddered at the monstrous atrocities of such law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Our Constitution, however, has not accepted this abso- lute supremacy of our <ORG> or <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus by article 245 (1) the legislative power is definitely made "subject to the provisions of this Constitution."
RatioOfTheDecision	Turning to the Constitution, article 13 (2) provides as follows:  "The <ORG> shall not make any law which takes away or abridges the rights conferred by this Part and any law made in contravention of this clause shall, to the extent of the cont ravention, be void."
RatioOfTheDecision	This clearly puts a definite limitation on the wide legislative powers given by article 246.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is certainly within the competency of the <ORG> to judge and declare whether there has been any contravention of this limitation.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this respect again the <ORG> has supremacy over the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	From the provisions so far referred to, it clearly follows that there are two principal limitations to the legislative power of parliament, namely,--  (i) that the law must be within the legislative compe- tence of parliament as prescribed by article 246; and  (ii) that such law must be subject to the pro-visions of the Constitution and must not take away or abridge the rights conferred by Part III.
RatioOfTheDecision	There can be no question--and, indeed, the learned Attorney-General does not contend otherwise--that both these matters are justiciable and it is open to the <ORG>s to decide whether <ORG> has transgressed either of the limitations upon its legislative power.
Argument	Learned counsel for the petitioner do es not say that the impugned Act is ultra vires the legislative powers of <ORG> as prescribed by article 246.
Argument	His contention is that the impugned Act is void because it takes away or abridges the fundamental rights of citizens conferred by Part III of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is, therefore, necessary to ascertain first the exact nature, extent and scope of the particular fundamental right insist- ed upon and then to see whether the impugned Act has taken away or, in any way, abridged the fundamental right so ascertained.
RatioOfTheDecision	Civil rights of a person are generally divided into two classes, namely, the rights attached to the person (jus personarum) and the rights to things, i.e., property (jus rerum).
RatioOfTheDecision	Of the rights attached to the person, the first and foremost is the freedom of life, which means the right to live, i.e., the right that one's life shall not be taken away except under authority of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Next to the freedom of life comes the freedom of the person, which means that one's body shall not be touched, vio lated, arrested or imprisoned and one's limbs shall not be injured or maimed except under authority of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The truth of the matter is that the right to live and the freedom of the person are the primary rights attached to the person.
RatioOfTheDecision	If a man's person is free, it is then and then only that he can exercise a variety of other auxiliary rights, that is to say, he can, within certain limits, speak what he likes, assemble where he likes, form any associations or unions, move about freely as his "own inclination may direct," reside and settle anywhere he likes and practise any profession or carry on any occupation, trade or business.
RatioOfTheDecision	These are attributes of the freedom of the person and are consequently rights attached to the person.
RatioOfTheDecision	It should be clearly borne in mind that these are not all the rights attached to the person.
RatioOfTheDecision	Besides them there are varieties of other rights which are also the attributes of the freedom of the person.
RatioOfTheDecision	All rights attached to the person are usually called personal liberties and they ar e too numerous to be enumerated.
RatioOfTheDecision	Some of these auxiliary rights are so important and fundamental that they are re- garded and valued as separate and independent rights apart from the freedom of the person.
RatioOfTheDecision	Personal liberties may be compendiously summed up as the right to do as one pleases within the law.
RatioOfTheDecision	I say within the law because liberty is not unbridled licence.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is what <NAME> called "regulated freedom."
RatioOfTheDecision	Said <NAME> in Book III, Ch.
RatioOfTheDecision	3, of his Spirit of the Laws:  "In Governments, that is, in societies directed by laws, liberty can consist only in the power of doing what we ought to will, and in not being constrained to do what we ought not to will.
RatioOfTheDecision	We must have continually present to our minds the difference between independence and liberty.
RatioOfTheDecision	Liberty is a right of doing whatever the laws permit, and if a citizen could do what they forbid, he would no longer be possessed of liberty, because all his fellow-citizens would enjoy the same power."
Precedent	To the same effect are the following observations  of <NAME> in his Works Vol.
Precedent	II, p. 393:  "Liberty is the creation of law, essentially different from that authorised licentiousness that trespasses on right.
Precedent	It is a legal and refined idea, the offspring of high civilization, which the savage never understands, and never can understand.
Precedent	Liberty exists in proportion to wholesome restraint; the more restraint on others to keep off from us, the more liberty we have.
Precedent	It is an error to suppose that liberty consists in a paucity of laws .........
Precedent	The working of our complex system, full of checks and restraints on legislative, executive and judicial power is favourable to liberty and justice.
Precedent	These checks and restraints are so many safeguards set around individual rights and interests.
Precedent	That man is free who is protected from injury."
Precedent	Therefore, putting restraint on the freedom of wrong doing of one person is really.
Precedent	securing the liberty of the intended victims.
Precedent	To curb the freedom of the saboteur of surreptitiously removing the fish plates from the railway lines i s to ensure the safety and liberty of movement of the numerous innocent and unsuspecting passengers.
Precedent	Therefore, restraints on liberty should be judged not only subjectively as applied to a few individuals who come within their opera- tions but also objectively as securing the liberty of a far greater number of individuals.
Precedent	Social interest in individu- al liberty may well have to be subordinated to other greater social interests.
Precedent	If a law ensures and protects the greater social interests then such law will be a wholesome and beneficent law although it may infringe the liberty of some individuals, for it will enure for the greater liberty of the rest of the members of the society.
Precedent	At the same time, our liberty has also to be guarded against executive, legislative as well as judicial usurpation of powers and prerogatives.
Precedent	Subject to certain restraints on individuals and reasonable checks on the <ORG> every person has a varie- ty of personal liberties too numerous to be cataloged.
Precedent	As will be seen more fully herea fter, our Constitution has recognised personal liberties as fundamental rights.
Precedent	It has guaranteed some of them under article 19 (1) but put re- straints on them by clauses (2) to (6).
Precedent	It has put checks on the <ORG>'s legislative powers by articles 21 and 22.
Precedent	It has by providing for preventive detention, recognised that individual liberty may be subordinated to the larger social interests.
Precedent	Turning now to the Constitution I find that Part III is headed and deals with "Fundamental Rights" under seven heads, besides, "General" provisions (articles 12 and 13), namely "Right to Equality" (articles 14 to 18), "Right to Freedom" (articles 19 to 22), "Right against Exploitation" (articles 23 and 24), "Right to Freedom of Religion" (articles 25 to 28), "Cultural and Educational Rights" (articles 29 and 30), "Right to Property" ( article 31 ), "Right to Constitutional Remedies" (articles 32 to 35).
Precedent	Under the heading "Right to Freedom" are grouped four arti- cles, 19 to 22.
Precedent	Article 19 (1) is in the following terms :--   " (1) All citizens shall have the right-  (a) to freedom of speech and expression;  (b) to assemble peaceably and without arms; (c) to form associations or unions;  (d) to move freely throughout the territory of India;  (e) to reside and settle in any part of the territory of India;  (f) to acquire, hold and dispose of property; and   (g) to practise any profession, or to carry on any occupation, trade or business."
Precedent	It will be noticed that of the seven rights protected by clause (1) of article 19 , six of them, namely, (a), (b),  (c), (d), (e) and (g) are what are said to be rights at- tached to the person (jus personarum).
RatioOfTheDecision	The remaining item, namely, (f) is the right to property (jus rerum).
RatioOfTheDecision	If there were nothing else in article 19 these rights would have been absolute rights and the protection given to them would have completely debarred parliament or any of <ORG> from making any law taking away or abridging any of those rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	But a perusal of article 19 makes it abun- dantly clear tha t none of the seven rights enumerated in clause (1) is an absolute right, for each of these rights is liable to be curtailed by laws made or to be made by the <ORG> to the extent mentioned in the several clauses (2) to (6) of that article.
RatioOfTheDecision	Those clauses save the power of the <ORG> to make laws imposing certain specified restrictions on the several rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	The nett result is that the unlimit- ed legislative power given by article 246 read with the different legislative lists in the Seventh Schedule is cut down by the provisions of article 19 and all laws made by the <ORG> with respect to these rights must, in order to be valid, observe these limitations.
RatioOfTheDecision	Whether any law has in fact transgressed these limitations is to be ascertained by the <ORG> and if in its view the restrictions imposed by the law are greater than what is permitted by clauses (2) to (6) whichever is applicable the <ORG> will declare the same to be unconstitutional and, therefore, void under article 13.
RatioOfTheDecision	Here again there is scope for the appli cation of the "intel- lectual yardstick" of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	If, however, the <ORG> finds, on scrutiny, that the law has not overstepped the constitutional limitations, the <ORG> will have to uphold the law, whether it likes the law or not.
RatioOfTheDecision	The first part of the argument is put broadly, namely, that personal liberty is generally guaranteed by the Consti- tution by article 19 (1) and that the Preventive Detention Act, 1950, has imposed unreasonable restrictions thereon in violation of the provisions of clauses (2) to (6) of that article.
RatioOfTheDecision	The very first question that arises, therefore, is as to whether the freedom of the person which is primarily and directly suspended or de- stroyed by preventive detention is at all governed by arti- cle 19 (1).
RatioOfTheDecision	If personal liberty as such is guaranteed by any of the sub-clauses of article 19 (1) then why has it also been protected by article 21 ?
RatioOfTheDecision	The answer suggested by learned counsel for the petitioner is that personal liberty as a substantive right is protected by article 19 (1 ) and article 21 gives only an additional protection by prescrib- ing the procedure according to which that right may be taken away.
RatioOfTheDecision	I am unable to accept this contention.
RatioOfTheDecision	If this argument were correct, then it would follow that our Consti- tution does not guarantee to any person, citizen or non- citizen, the freedom of his life as a substantive right at all, for the substantive right to life does not fall within any of the sub-clauses of clause (1) of article 19.
Precedent	It is retorted in reply that no constitution or human laws can guarantee life which is the gift of God who alone can guar- antee and protect it.
Precedent	On a parity of reasoning no Constitu- tion or human laws can in that sense guarantee freedom of speech or free movement, for one may be struck dumb by disease or may lose the use of his legs by paralysis or as a result of amputation.
Precedent	Further, what has been called the procedural protection of article 21 would be an act of supererogation, for when God takes away one's life, whatever opportunity He may have ha d given to <NAME> to explain his conduct before sending him down, He is not likely in these degenerate-days to observe the requirements of notice or fair trial before any human tribunal said to be required by article 21.
Precedent	The fifth Amendment and the Fourteenth Amendment of the American Constitution give specific protection to life as a substantive right.
Precedent	So does article 31 of the Japanese Constitution of 1946.
Precedent	There is no reason why our Constitution should not do the same.
Precedent	The truth is that article 21 has given that protection to life as a substan- tive right and that, as will be seen hereafter, that article properly understood does not purport to prescribe any par- ticular procedure at all.
Precedent	The  further astounding result of the argument of counsel for the petitioner will be that the citizen of India will have only the rights enumerated in article 19 , clause (1) and no other right attached to his person.
RatioOfTheDecision	As I have already stated, besides the several rights mentioned in the several sub- clauses of article 19 (1)  there are many other personal liberties which a free man, i.e., a man who has the freedom of his person, may exercise.
RatioOfTheDecision	Some of those other rights have been referred to by <NAME> of Calcutta in his unreported judgment in Miscellaneous Case No.
RatioOfTheDecision	166 of 1950 (<NAME> v. The <NAME>f Secretary of West Bengal) while referring the case to <ORG> in the following words :--  "It must be remembered that a free man has far more and wider rights than those stated in article 19 (1) of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	For example, a free man can eat what he likes subject to rationing laws, work as much as he likes or idle as much as he likes.
Precedent	He can drink anything he likes subject to the licensing laws and smoke and do a hundred and one things which are not included in article 19.
Precedent	If freedom of person was the result of article 19 , then a free man would only have the seven rights mentioned in that article.
Precedent	But obviously the free man in India has far greater rights."
Precedent	I find myself in complete agreement with the learned <NAME> f Justice on this point.
Precedent	If it were otherwise, the citizen's right to eat what he likes will be liable to be taken away by the executive fiat of the Civil Supply Depart- ment without the necessity of any rationing laws.
Precedent	The Government may enforce prohibition without any prohibition laws or licensing laws and so on.
Precedent	I cannot accept that our Constitution intended to give no protection to the bundle of rights which, together with the rights mentioned in sub- clauses (a) to (e) and (g) make up personal liberty.
Precedent	In- deed, I regard it as a merit of our Constitution that it does not attempt to enumerate exhaustively all the personal rights but uses the compendious expression "personal liber- ty" in' article 21 , and protects all of them.
Precedent	It is pointed out that in the original draft the word "liberty" only was used as in the American Constitution but <ORG> added the word "personal" to make it clear that what was being protected by what is now article 21 was not what had already been pro- tected by wh at is now article 19.
Precedent	If it were permissible to refer to <ORG> report, it would be anoth- er answer to the contentions of learned counsel for the petitioner that personal liberty as a substantive right was protected by article 19.
Precedent	I do not, however, desire to base my judgment on <ORG> report and I express no opinion as to its admissibility.
Precedent	Whatever the intentions of <ORG> might have been, the Constitution as finally passed has in article 21 used the words "personal liberty" which have a definite connotation in law as I have explained.
Precedent	It does not mean only liberty of the person but it means liberty or the rights attached to the person (jus personarum).
Precedent	The expressions "freedom of life" or "personal liberty" are not to be found in article 19 and it is strain- ing the language of article 19 to squeeze in personal liber- ty into that article.
Precedent	In any case the right to life cannot be read into article 19.
Precedent	Article 19 being confined, in its operation, to cit izens only, a non-citizen will have no protection for his life and personal liberty except what has been called the procedural protection of article 21.
Precedent	If there be no substantive right what will the procedure protect ?
Precedent	I recognise that it is not imperative that a foreigner should have the same privileges as are given to a citizen, but if article 21 is construed in the way I have suggested even a foreigner will have equal protection for his life and personal liberty before the laws of our country under our Constitution.
Precedent	I am unable, there- fore, for all the reasons given above, to agree that person- al liberties are the result of article 19 or that that article purports to protect all of them.
Precedent	It is next urged that the expression "personal liberty" is synonymous with the right to move freely and, therefore, comes directly under article 19 (1) (d).
Precedent	Reference is made to the unreported dissenting judgment of Sen <NAME> in Miscellaneous Case No.
Precedent	166 of 1950 while referring that case to <ORG>.
Precedent	298   In his judgment <NAME> quoted the following passage from <NAME>'s Commentaries :--  "Next to personal security the law of :England regards, asserts and preserves, the personal liberty of individuals.
Precedent	This personal liberty consists in the power of locomotion, of changing situation, or moving one's person to whatsoever place one's own inclination may direct, without imprisonment or restraint, unless by due course of law."
Precedent	(Page 73 of <NAME> Edition (4th Edition) of <NAME>, Book I, Chapter I.
Precedent	On the authority of the above passage the learned Judge concluded that personal liberty came within article 19 (1)(d).
Precedent	I am unable to agree with the learned Judge's con- clusion.
Precedent	On a perusal of Chapter I of Book I of Black- stone's Commentaries it will appear that the]earned commen- tator divided the rights attached to the person (jus person- arum) into two classes, namely, "personal security" and "personal liberty."
Precedent	Under the head "personal security" <NAME> included several rights, namely, the right s to' life, limb, body, health and reputation, and under the head "personal liberty" he placed only the right of free move- ment.
Precedent	He first dealt with the several rights classified by him under the head "personal security" and then proceeded to say that next to those rights came personal liberty which according to his classification consisted only in the right of free locomotion.
Precedent	There is no reason to suppose that in article 21 of our Constitution the expression "personal liberty" has been used in the restricted sense in which <NAME> used it in his Commentaries.
Precedent	If "personal liber- ty" in article 21 were synonymous with the right to move freely which is mentioned in article 19 (1) (d ), then the astounding result will be that only the last mentioned right has what has been called the procedural protection of arti- cle 21 but none of the other rights in the other sub-clauses of article 19 (1) has any procedural protection at all.
Precedent	According to learned counsel for the petitioner the proce- dure required by ar ticle 21 consists of notice and a right of hearing before an impartial tribunal.
Precedent	Therefore, accord- ing to him, a man's right of movement cannot be taken away without giving him notice and a fair trial before an impartial tribunal but he may be deprived of his freedom of speech or his property or any of his other rights without the formality of any procedure at all.
Precedent	The proposi- tion has only to be stated to be rejected.
Precedent	In my judgment, article '19 protects some of the important attributes of personal liberty as independent rights and the expression "personal liberty" has been 'used in article 21 as a compen- dious term including within its meaning all the varieties of rights which go to make up the personal liberties of men.
Precedent	Learned counsel for the petitioner next contends that personal liberty undoubtedly means or includes the freedom of the person and the pith and substance of the freedom of the person is right to move about freely and consequently a preventive detention law which destroys or suspends the  freedom of the person must inevitably destroy or suspend the right of free movement and must necessarily offend against the protection given to the citizen by article 19 (1)(d) unless it satisfies the test of reasonableness laid down in clause (5).
Precedent	The argument is attractive and requires serious consideration as to the exact purpose and scope of sub- clause (d) of article 19 (1).
Precedent	There are indications in the very language of article 19 (1) (d) itself that its purpose is to protect not the gener- al right of free movement which emanates from the freedom of the person but only a specific and 'limited aspect of it, namely, the special right of a free citizen of India to move freely throughout the Indian territory, i.e., from one State to another within the <ORG>.
Precedent	In other words, it guarantees, for example, that a free Indian citizen ordinarily residing in the State of West Bengal will be free to move from West Bengal to Bihar or to reside and settle in Madras or the Punjab without any let or hindrance other th an as provided in clause (5).
Precedent	It is this special right of movement of the Indian citizen in this specific sense and for this particu- lar purpose which is protected by article 19 (1) (d).
Precedent	It is argued on the authority of a decision of <ORG> of <ORG> presided over by <NAME> in <CITATION_245155> (1) that the words "through-.
Precedent	out the territory of India" occurring in that sub-clause only indicate that our Constitution does not guarantee to its citizens the right of free movement in or into foreign territory and that those words have been added to save passport restrictions.
Precedent	I am unable to accept this interpre- tation.
Precedent	Our Constitution cannot possibly give to any of its citizens any right of free movement in a foreign country and it was wholly superfluous to specifically indicate this in the Constitution, for that would have gone without saying.
Precedent	The words "throughout the territory of India" are not used in connection with most of the other sub-clauses of clause (1) of article 19.
Precedent	Does s uch omission indicate that our Constitution guarantees to its citizens freedom of speech and expression, say, in Pakistan ?
Precedent	Does it guarantee to.
Precedent	its citizens a right to assemble or to form associations or unions in a foreign territory ?
Precedent	Clearly not.
Precedent	Therefore, it was not necessary to use those words in sub-clause (d) to indicate that free movement in foreign countries was not being guaranteed.
Precedent	It is said that by the use of those words the Constitution makes it clear that no1 guarantee was being given to any citizen with regard to emigration from India without a passport and that the freedom of movement was restricted within the territory of India.
Precedent	Does the omission of those words from article 19 (1) (a) indicate that the citizen of India has been guaranteed such freedom of speech and expression as will enable him to set up a broadcasting station and broadcast his views and expressions to foreign lands without a licences ?
Precedent	Clearly not.
Precedent	Dropping this line of argument and adopting a totally new line of argument  it is said that by the use of the words "throughout the territory of India" the Constitution indicates that the widest right of free movement that it could possibly give to its citizens has been given.
Precedent	Does.
Precedent	then, the omission of those words from the other subclauses indicate that the Constitution has kept back some parts of those rights even beyond the limits of the qualifying clauses that follow ?
Precedent	Do not those other rights prevail throughout the Indian territory ?
Precedent	(1) 54 C.W.N.
Precedent	394.
Precedent	301  Clearly they do, even without those words.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, those words must have been used in sub-clause (d) for -some other purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	That other purpose, as far as I can apprehend it, is to indicate that free movement from one State to another within the Union is protected so that <ORG> may not by a law made under Entry 81 in List I curtail it beyond the limits prescribed by clause (5) of article 19.
RatioOfTheDecision	Its purpose, as I read it, is not to provide protection for the general right of free movement but to secure a specific an d special right of the Indian citizen to move freely throughout the territories of India regarded as an independent additional right apart from the general right of locomotion emanating from the freedom of the person.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is a guarantee against unfair discrimination in the matter of free movement of the Indian citizen throughout <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	In short, it is a protection against provincialism.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has nothing to do with the freedom of the person as such.
RatioOfTheDecision	That is guaranteed to every person, citizen or otherwise, in the manner and to the extent formulated by article 21.
RatioOfTheDecision	Clause (5) of article 19 qualifies sub-clause (d) of clause (1) which should, therefore, be read in the light of clause (5).
RatioOfTheDecision	The last mentioned clause permits the <ORG> to impose reasonable restrictions on the exercise of the right of free movement throughout the territory of India as ex- plained above.
RatioOfTheDecision	Imposition of reasonable restrictions clearly implies that the right of free movement is not entirely destroyed but that parts of the right  remain.
RatioOfTheDecision	This reasona- ble restriction can be imposed either in the interest of the general public or for the protection of the interests of any Scheduled Tribe.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Scheduled Tribes usually reside in what are called the Scheduled Areas.
RatioOfTheDecision	The provision for imposing restriction on the citizens' right of free movement in the interests of the Scheduled Tribes clearly indicates that the restriction is really on his right of free movement into or within the Scheduled Areas.
RatioOfTheDecision	It means that if it be found necessary for the protection of the Scheduled Tribes the citizens may be restrained from entering into or moving about in the Scheduled Areas, although they are left quite free to move about elsewhere.
RatioOfTheDecision	This restraint may well be necessary for the protection of the members of the, Sched- uled Tribes who are generally impecunious and constitute a backward class.
RatioOfTheDecision	They may need protection against money- lenders or others who may be out to exploit them.
RatioOfTheDecision	They may have to be protected against their own impecunious habits whi ch may result in their selling or mortgaging their hearths and homes.
RatioOfTheDecision	Likewise, the free movement of citizens may have to be restricted in the interest of the general public.
RatioOfTheDecision	A person suffering from an infectious disease may be prevent from moving about and spreading the disease.
RatioOfTheDecision	and regulations for his segregation in the nature of quarantine may have to be introduced.
RatioOfTheDecision	Likewise, healthy people may be prevented, in the interests of the general public, from entering a plague-infected area.
RatioOfTheDecision	There may be protected places, e.g., forts or other strategic places, access where- to may have to be regulated or even prohibited in the inter- ests of the general public.
RatioOfTheDecision	The point to be noted, however, is that when free movement is thus restricted, whether in the interest of the general public or for the protection of the Scheduled Tribes, such restriction has reference gener- ally to a certain local area which becomes the prohibited area but the right of free movement in all other areas in the Union is left unimpaired.
RatioOfTheDecision	Th e circumstance that clause (5) contemplates only the taking away of a specified area and thereby restricting the field of the exercise of the right conferred by subclause (d) of clause (1) indicates to my mind that subclause (d)is concerned, not with the freedom of the person or the general right of free movement but with a specific aspect of it regarded as an independent right apart from the freedom of the person.
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words in sub-clause (d)the real emphasis is on the words "throughout the territory of India."
RatioOfTheDecision	The purpose of article 19 (1) (d) is to guarantee that there shall be no State barrier.
RatioOfTheDecision	It gives protection against provincialism.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has nothing to do with the freedom of the person as such.
RatioOfTheDecision	Finally, the ambit and scope of the rights protected by article 19 (1) have to be considered.
RatioOfTheDecision	Does it protect the right of free movement and the other personal rights therein mentioned in all circumstances irrespective of any other consideration ?
RatioOfTheDecision	Does it not postulate a capacity to exercise the rights ?
RatioOfTheDecision	Doe s its protection continue even though the citizen lawfully loses his capacity for exercising those rights ?
RatioOfTheDecision	How can the continuance of those personal rights be compatible with the lawful detention of the person ?
RatioOfTheDecision	These personal rights and lawful detention cannot go together.
Precedent	Take the case of a person who has been properly convicted of an offence punish- able under a section of the Indian Penal Code as to the reasonableness of which there is no dispute.
Precedent	His right to freedom of speech is certainly impaired.
Precedent	Under clause (2) the <ORG> may make a law relating to libel, slander, defama- tion, contempt of <ORG> or any matter which offends against decency or morality or which undermines the security of, or tends to overthrow, the <ORG>.
Precedent	Any law on any of these matters contemplated by this clause certainly must have some direct reference to speech and expression.
Precedent	It means that the law may directly curtail the freedom of speech so that the citizen may not talk libel or speak contemptuously of the <ORG> or express inde cent or immoral sentiments by speech or other forms of expression or utter seditious words.
Precedent	To say that every crime undermines the security of the <ORG> and, therefore, every section of the Indian Penal Code , irrespective of whether it has any reference to speech or expression, is a law within the meaning of this clause is wholly unconvincing and betrays only a vain and forlorn attempt to find an explanation for meeting the argument that any conviction by a Court of law must necessarily infringe article 19 (1) (a).
Precedent	-There can be no getting away from the fact that a detention as a result of a conviction impairs the freedom of speech far beyond what is permissible under clause (2) of article 19.
Precedent	Likewise a detention on lawful conviction impairs each of the other personal rights men- tioned in sub-clauses (b) to (e) and (g) far beyond the limits of clauses (8) to (6).
Precedent	The argument that every section of the Indian Penal Code irrespective of whether it has any reference to any of the rights referred to in sub- cl auses (b) to (e) and (g) is a law imposing reasonable restriction on those several rights has not even the merit of plausibility.
Precedent	There can be no doubt that a detention as a result of lawful conviction must necessari- ly impair the fundamental personal rights guaranteed by article 19 (1) far beyond what is permissible under clauses (2) to (6) of that article and yet nobody can think of questioning the validity of the detention or of the section of the Indian Penal Code under which the sentence was passed.
Precedent	Why ?
Precedent	Because the freedom of his person having been lawfully taken away, the convict ceases to be entitled to exercise the freedom of speech and expression or any of the other personal rights protected by clause (1) of article  19.
Precedent	On a parity of reasoning he cannot, while the detention lasts, exercise any other personal right, e.g., he cannot eat what he likes or when he likes but has to eat what the Jail Code provides for him and at the time when he is by Jail regulations required to eat.
Precedent	Therefore, the co nclusion is irresistible that the rights protected by article 19 (1 ), in so far as they relate to rights attached to the person, i.e., the rights referred to in sub-clauses (a) to (e) and  (g), are rights which only a free citizen, who has the freedom of his person unimpaired, can exercise.
Precedent	It is pointed out, as a counter to the above reasonings, that detention as a result of a lawful conviction does not deprive a person of his right to acquire or hold or dispose of his property mentioned in sub-clause (f).
Precedent	The answer is simple, namely, that that right is not a right attached to the person (jus personrum) and its existence is not depend- ent on the freedom of the person.
Precedent	Loss of freedom of the person, therefore, does not suspend the right to property.
Precedent	But suppose a person loses his property by reason of its having been compulsorily acquired under article 31 he loses his right to hold that property and cannot complain that his fundamental right under sub-clause (f) of clause (1) of article 19 has been infring ed.
Precedent	It follows that the rights enumerated in article 19 (1) subsist while the citizen has the legal capacity to exercise them.
Precedent	If his capacity to exercise them is gone, by reason of a lawful conviction with respect to the rights in sub-clauses (a) to (e) and (g), or by reason of a lawful compulsory acquisition with respect to the right in sub- clause (f), he ceases to have those rights while his inca- pacity lasts.
Precedent	It further follows that if a citizen's free- dom of the person is lawfully taken away otherwise than as a result of a lawful conviction for an offence, that citizen, for precisely the same reason, cannot exercise any of the rights attached to his person including those enumerated in sub-clauses (a) to (e) and (g) of article 19 (1).
Precedent	In my judgment a lawful detention, whether punitive or preventive, does not offend against the protection conferred by article 19 (1) (a) to (e) and (g), for those rights must necessarily cease when the freedom of the person is lawfully taken away.
Precedent	In short, those rights  end where the lawful detention be- gins.
Precedent	So construed, article 19 and article 21 may, there- fore, easily go together and there is, in reality, no con- flict between them.
RatioOfTheDecision	It follows, therefore, that the validi- ty or otherwise of preventive detention does not depend on, and is not dealt with by, article 19.
RatioOfTheDecision	To summarise, the freedom of the person is not the result of article 19.
Precedent	Article 19 only deals with' certain particu- lar rights which, in their origin and inception, are attributes of the freedom of the person but being of great importance are regarded as specific and independent rights.
Precedent	It does not deal with the freedom of the person as such.
Precedent	Article 19 (1) (d) protects a specific aspect of the right of free locomotion, namely, the right to move freely throughout the territory of India which is regarded as a special privilege or right of an Indian citizen and is protected as such.
Precedent	The protection of article 19 is co-termi- nous with the legal capacity of a citizen to exercise the rights protected the reby, for sub-clauses (a) to (e) and (g) of article 19 (1) postulate the freedom of the person which alone can ensure the capacity to exercise the rights pro- tected by those sub-clauses.
Precedent	A citizen who loses the free- dom of his person by being lawfully detained, whether as a result of a conviction for an offence or as a result of preventive detention loses his capacity to exercise those rights and, therefore, has none of the rights which sub- clauses (a) to (e) and (g) may protect.
Precedent	306  In my judgment article 19 has no bearing on the question of the validity or otherwise of preventive detention and, that being so, clause (5) which prescribes a test of reasonable- ness to be defined and applied by the <ORG> has no applica- tion at all.
Precedent	Article 19 being thus out of the way, I come to article 20 which is concerned with providing protection against what are well known as ex post facto laws, double jeopardy and self-incrimination.
Precedent	This article constitutes a limitation on the absolute legislative power which wo uld, but for this article, be exercisable by <ORG> or the State Legisla- tures under article 246 read with the legislative lists.
Precedent	If the <ORG> disobeys this limitation the <ORG> will certainly prevent it.
Precedent	Article 20 has no bearing on preven- tive detention laws and I pass on.
Precedent	Article 21 runs thus:  "21.
Precedent	No person shall be deprived of his life or person- al liberty except according to procedure established by law."
Precedent	The contention of learned counsel for the petitioner is that by this article the Constitution offers to every per- son, citizen or non-citizen, only a procedural protection.
Precedent	According to the argument, this article does not purport to give any protection to life or personal liberty as a sub- stantive right but only prescribes a procedure that must be followed before a person may be deprived of his life or personal liberty.
Precedent	I am unable to accept this contention.
Precedent	Article 21 , as the marginal note states, guarantees to every person "protection of life and personal liberty."
Precedent	As I read it, it  defines the substantive fundamental right to which protection is given and does not purport to prescribe any particular procedure at all.
Precedent	That a person shall not be deprived of his life or personal liberty except according to procedure established by law is the substantive fundamental right to which protection is given by the Constitution.
Precedent	The avowed object of the article, as I apprehend it, is to define the ambit of the right to life and personal liberty which is to be protected as a fundamental right.
Precedent	The right to life and personal liberty protected by article 21 is not an absolute right but is a qualified right--a right circumscribed by the possibility or risk of being lost according to procedure established by law.
Precedent	Liability to deprivation according to procedure established by law is in the nature of words of limitation.
Precedent	The article delimits the right by a reference to its liability to deprivation according to procedure estab- lished by law and by this very definition throws a corre- sponding obligation  on the <ORG> to follow a procedure before depriving a man of his life and personal liberty.
Precedent	What that procedure is to be is not within the purpose or purview of this article to prescribe or indicate.
Precedent	The claim of learned counsel for the petitioner is that article 21 prescribes a procedure.
Precedent	This procedure, accord- ing to learned counsel, means those fundamental immutable rules of procedure which are sanctioned or well established by principles of natural justice accepted in all climes and countries and at all times.
Precedent	Apart from the question whether any rule of natural procedure exists which conforms to the notions of justice and fair play of all mankind at all times, it has to be ascertained whether the language of article 21 will permit its introduction into our Constitu- tion.
Precedent	The question then arises as to what is the meaning of the expression "procedure established by law."
Precedent	The word "procedure" in article 21 must be taken to signify some step or method or manner of proceeding leading up to the depriva- tio n of life or personal liberty.
Precedent	According to the language used in the article, this procedure has to be "established by law."
Precedent	The word "establish" according to the Oxford English Dictionary, Vol.
RatioOfTheDecision	III, p. 297, means, amongst other things, "to render stable or firm ; to strengthen by materi- al support; to fix, settle, institute or ordain permanently by enactment or agreement."
RatioOfTheDecision	According to Dr. <NAME>'s edition of the New Gresham Dictionary the word "establish," means, amongst other things, "to found permanently; to institute; to enact or decree; to ordain; to ratify; to make firm."
RatioOfTheDecision	It follows that the word "established" in its ordi- nary natural sense means, amongst other things, "enacted."
RatioOfTheDecision	"Established by law" will, therefore, mean "enacted by law."
RatioOfTheDecision	If this sense of the word "established" is accepted, then the word "law" must mean <ORG>-made law and cannot possibly mean.
RatioOfTheDecision	the principles of natural justice, for no procedure can be said to have ever been "enacted" by those principles.
RatioOfTheDecision	When section 124-A of the  Indian Penal Code speaks of "Government established by law," surely it does not mean "Government set up by natural justice."
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, procedure established by law must, I apprehend, be procedure enacted by the <ORG> which, by its definition in article 12 , includes parliament.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no escape from this position if the cardinal rule of construc- tion, namely, to give the words used in a statute their ordinary natural meaning, is applied.
RatioOfTheDecision	And this construction introduces no novelty or innovation, for at the date of the Constitution the law of procedure in this country.
RatioOfTheDecision	both civil and criminal, was mainly if not wholly, the creature of statute.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Hindu or Muhammadan laws of procedure were abrogated and replaced by the Code of Civil Procedure or the Code of Criminal Procedure.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, procedure established by law is quite compatible with procedure enact- ed by law.
RatioOfTheDecision	If, however, the word "established" is taken to mean "sanctioned" or "settled" or "made firm" then the question will arise as to the meanin g of the word "law" in that context.
RatioOfTheDecision	Reference is made to Salmond's Jurisprudence, 10th Edition, p. 37, showing that the term "law" is used in two senses and it is suggested that the word "law" in the expression "established by law "means law in its abstract sense of the principles of natural justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is "jus" and not "lex," says learned counsel for the petitioner.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is pointed out that both the English and the Indian law in many cases, some of which have been cited before us, have recog- nised and applied the principles of natural justice and that this <ORG> should do the same in interpreting tim provisions of our Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	I find it difficult to let in princi- ples of natural justice as being within the meaning of the word "law," having regard to the obvious meaning of that word in the other articles.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 14 certainly embodies a principle of natural justice which ensures to.
RatioOfTheDecision	309  every person equality before the law.
RatioOfTheDecision	When natural jus- tice speaks of and enjoins equality- before the law, that la w must refer to something outside natural justice, and must mean the <ORG>-made laws.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is only when the <ORG> law gives equality to every person that that law is said to be in accordance with natural justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	There can be no doubt that the words "in accordance with law" in article 17 have reference to <ORG> law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Likewise, the word "law" in article 20 (1) can mean nothing but law made by the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The same remark applies to the words "in accordance with law" in articles 23, 31 and 32.
RatioOfTheDecision	Natural justice does not impose any tax and, therefore, the word "law" in articles 265 and 286 must mean <ORG>-made law.
RatioOfTheDecision	If this be the correct meaning of the word "law" then there is no scope for intro- ducing the principles of natural justice in article 21 and "procedure established by law" must mcan procedure estab- lished by law made by the <ORG> which, as defined, includes <ORG> and the Legislatures of the <ORG>s.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have been referred to a number of text books and decisions showing the development of the American do ctrine of "due process of law" and we have been urged to adopt those principles in our Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The matter has to be considered against its historical background.
RatioOfTheDecision	The English settlers in different parts of America had carried with them the English common law as a sort of personal law regulating their rights and liberties inter se as well as between them and the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	After the War of Independence the Constitu- tions of the United <ORG>s were drawn up in writing.
Precedent	The majority of those who framed the Constitution were lawyers and had closely studied the Commentaries of the great Eng- lish jurist <NAME>, who in his famous commentaries had advocated the separation of the three limbs of the <ORG>, namely, the executive, the legislature and the judiciary.
Precedent	<NAME>'s Spirit of Laws had already been published wherein he gave a broader and more emphatic expression to the Aristotelian doctrine of separation of powers.
Precedent	The experience of the repressive laws of <ORG> had im- pressed upon the framers of th e American Constitution the belief that it was the habit of all legislative bodies to grasp and exercise powers that did not belong to them.
Precedent	The interference of the colonial governors with legislation and the judiciary was also real.
Precedent	This sad experience coupled with the political philosophy of the time induced the fram- ers of the American Constitutions to adopt safeguards not only against the executive but also against the legislature.
Precedent	(See Munro on the Government of the United States, 5th Edition, Chapter IV, p. 53 et seq.).
Precedent	Says Judge <NAME> in his Constitutional Limitations, 6th Edition, Vol.
Precedent	II, Chap- ter XI, p. 755:  "The people of the American States, holding the sover- eignty in their own hands, have no occasion to exact any pledges from any one for a due observation of individual rights; but the aggressive tendency of power is such that they have deemed it of no small importance, that, in framing the instruments under which their governments are to be administered by their agents, they should repeat  and re- enact this guarantee, and thereby adopt it as a principle of constitutional protection."
RatioOfTheDecision	There can be little doubt that the people of the differ- ent States in America intended not to take any risk as to their life, liberty or property even from the legislature.
RatioOfTheDecision	As <NAME> puts it at pp.
RatioOfTheDecision	58-61 :--  "The framers of the Constitution set boundaries to the powers of the <ORG>, and it was their intent that these limitations should be observed.
RatioOfTheDecision	But how was such observance to be enforced by the <ORG> ?
RatioOfTheDecision	The statesmen of 1767 did not categorically answer that question."
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution was silent and there was no express provision as to who was to serve as umpire in case the <ORG> overstepped the limits of its legislative powers.
RatioOfTheDecision	By the 5th Amendment what is now known as the "due process clause" was introduced in the Federal Constitution and by the 14th Amendment a similar clause was adopted in the State Constitutions.
RatioOfTheDecision	Some of the State Constitutions used the words "due course of law," some repeated th e words of Magna Charta, namely, "the law of the land" but most of them used the expression "due process of law."
RatioOfTheDecision	All the expressions meant the same thing, namely, that no person should be deprived of his life, liberty or property except in due process of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution by this clause gave <ORG> an opportunity to take upon itself the function of declaring the national laws unconstitutional.
RatioOfTheDecision	And <ORG>, under the leadership of Chief Justice <NAME>, seized this opportunity and assumed the right to say the last word on questions of constitutionality, and possesses that right to-day: (<NAME>, p. 62).
RatioOfTheDecision	The expression "due process of law" has been interpreted by <ORG> in different ways at different times.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> in his book on the Growth of Constitutional Power in the United States at p. 107 says, with reference to the development of the doctrine of due procedure:  "The American history of its interpretation falls into three periods.
RatioOfTheDecision	During the first peri od covering roughly the first century of <ORG> under the Constitution "due process" was interpreted "principally as a restriction upon procedure--and largely the judicial procedure--by which the <ORG> exercised its powers.
RatioOfTheDecision	During the second period,which, again roughly speaking, extended through 1936, "due process" was expanded to serve as a restriction not merely upon procedure but upon the substance of the activi- ties in which the <ORG> might engage.
RatioOfTheDecision	During the third period extending from 1936 to date, the use of "due process" as a substantive restriction has been largely suspended or abandoned, leaving it principally in its original status as a restriction upon procedure."
RatioOfTheDecision	In the guise of interpreting "due process of law" <ORG> went much further than even Lord <NAME> ever thought of doing.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> gradually arrogated to themselves the power to revise all legislations.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the beginning they confined themselves to insisting on a due procedure to be followed before a pe <NAME> was deprived of his life, liberty or property.
RatioOfTheDecision	In course of time, "due process of law" came to be applied to pe<NAME>al liberty, to social control, to procedure  to jurisdiction and to substantive law: (<NAME>, p. 642).
RatioOfTheDecision	In the words of <NAME> "due process of law" became a sort of palladium covering all manner of individual rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	A_II the while <ORG> refused to define the phrase, but used it to enable it to declare unconstitutional any Act of legislation which it thought unreasonable: (<NAME>, p.  657).
Precedent	In <NAME> v. <NAME> (1) we find the following observa- tions:  "This Court has never attempted to define with precision the words' due process of law ................
Precedent	It is suffi- cient to say that there are certain immutable principles of justice which inhere in the very idea of free government which no member of the Union may disregard.
Precedent	"In <NAME> v. <NAME> (2) <NAME> observed:  "The words 'by the law of the land' as used in the Constitution, do not mean a statute passed for the purpose of wo rking the wrong.
RatioOfTheDecision	That construction would render the restriction absolutely nugatory, and turn this part of the Constitution into mere nonsense.
RatioOfTheDecision	The people would be made to say to the two <ORG>: ' You shall be vested with the legis- lative power of the.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG>, but no one shall be disenfran- chised or deprived of any of the rights or privileges of a citizen, unless you pass a statute for that purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words you shall not do the wrong unless you choose to do it.'"
RatioOfTheDecision	It was thus that <ORG> of the United <ORG>s firmly established its own supremacy over the other two limbs of the <ORG>, namely, the executive and the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the words of <NAME> quoted in <NAME> at p. 61, "The Judges of Aragon began by setting aside laws and ended by making them."
RatioOfTheDecision	And all this sweeping development could only be possible because of the presence of one little word "due" which, in its content, knows no bound and is not subject to any fixed definition.
Precedent	Whenever a substantive law or some procedure laid dow n in any law did not find favour with the majority of the learned Judges of <ORG> it was not reasonableand, therefore, it was not "due."
Precedent	(1) 169 U.S. 366 at p. 389.
Precedent	(2) 4 Hill 140,  145.
Precedent	The very large and nebulous import of the word "due" was bound to result in anomalies, for what was not "due" on one day according to the Judges then constituting <ORG> became "due" say 20 years later according to the new Judges who then came to occupy the Bench, for the <ORG> had to adapt the Constitution to the needs of the society which were continually changing and growing.
Precedent	The larger content of due process of law, which included both procedural and substantive due process of law, had of necessity to be narrowed down, for social interest in personal liberty had to give way to social interest in other matters which came to be considered to be of more vital interest to the commu- nity.
Precedent	This was achieved by <ORG> of the United States evolving the new doctrine of police powers--a pecul- iar ly American doctrine.
Precedent	The police powers are nowhere exhaustively defined.
Precedent	In Chicago B.
Precedent	& Q. Ry.
Precedent	v. Drainage Commissioner (1) ,, police power" has been stated to "em- brace regulations designed to promote the public convenience or the general prosperity, as well as regulations designed to promote the public health, the public morals or the public safety."
Precedent	Reference in this connection may be made to Cooley's Constitutional Limitations, 8th Edition, Vol.
Precedent	II, p. 1223 and to Chapter XXVI of Willis at p. 727.
RatioOfTheDecision	The nett result is that the all-inclusive and indefina- ble doctrine of due process of law has in America now been brought back to its original status of a procedural due process of law by the enunciation and application of the new doctrine of police power as an antidote or palliative to the former.
RatioOfTheDecision	Who knows when the pendulum will swing again.
RatioOfTheDecision	Turning now to what has been called the procedural due process of law it will be found that the matter has been described in different languages in different cases.
Precedent	In  <NAME> v. <NAME> (2) <NAME> defined it thus:  "Due process of law undoubtedly means, in the due course of legal proceedings, according to those rules 204 u.s. 561,592.
Precedent	(2) 12 N.Y. 202.
Precedent	314  and forms which have been established for the protection of private rights."
Precedent	A more specific definition of the expression "the law of the land" meaning procedural due process was given by Web- ster appearing as counsel for the plaintiff in error in the Trustees of <ORG> v. <NAME> (1):  "By the law of the land is most clearly intended the general law; a law which hears before it condemns; which proceeds upon inquiry and renders judgment only after trial.
Precedent	The meaning is that every citizen shall hold his life, liberty, property, and.
Precedent	immunities, under the protection of the general rules which govern society.
Precedent	Everything which may pass under the form of an enactment is not therefore to be considered the law of the land."
Precedent	<NAME> in Ch.
