Precedent	XXIII, p. 661, says:  "The guarantee of due process of law as a matte r of procedure means that no part of a person's personal liberty, including ownership, shall be taken away from him except by the observance of certain formalities.
Precedent	Hence its object is the protection of the social interest in personal liberty."
Precedent	At p. 662 <NAME> enumerates the requirements of the procedural due process of law as follows:(1) notice.
Precedent	(2) opportunity to be heard, (3) an impartial tribunal, and (4) an orderly course of procedure.
Precedent	In short, the procedural due process requires that a person who is to be deprived of his life, liberty or property shall have had "his day in Court."
Precedent	This according to <NAME> by p. 736, means:  "(1) that he shall have had due notice, which may be actual or constructive, of the institution of the proceed- ings by which his legal rights may be affected; (2) that he shall be given a reasonable opportunity to appear and defend his rights, including the right himself to testify, to produce witnesses, and to introduce relevant documents and other evidence, (3) that the tribu nal in or before which his rights are adjudicated is so constituted as to give reasona- ble assurance of its.
Precedent	(1) 4 Wheaton 518 at p. 579; 4 L. Edn.
Precedent	629 at p. 645.
RatioOfTheDecision	315  honesty and impartiality; and (4) that it is a Court of competent jurisdiction."
RatioOfTheDecision	It will be noticed that the fourth item of <NAME> is different from the fourth item of <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such, in short, are the history of the development of the doctrine of the process of law in the United States and the requirements of the procedural due process as insisted on by <ORG> of that country.
RatioOfTheDecision	Learned counsel for the petitioner before us does not contend that we should import this American doctrine of due process of law in its full glory but that we should adopt the procedural part of it and insist that no person shall be deprived of his life or personal liberty except by the observance of the formalities which justice and fair play require to be observed.
RatioOfTheDecision	The arguments of learned counsel for the petitioner are attractive and in the first blu sh certainly appeal to our sentiment but on serious reflection I find several insuperable objections to the introduction of the American doctrine of procedural due process of law into our Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	That doctrine can only thrive and work where the legislature is subordinate to the judiciary in the sense that the latter can sit in judgment over and review all acts of the legislature.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such a doctrine can have no application to a field where the legislature is supreme.
RatioOfTheDecision	That is why the doctrine of "due process of law" is quite different in England where <ORG> is supreme.
Precedent	This difference is pointedly described by <NAME> in <NAME> v. People of California (1) at p. 531:  "The concessions of <ORG> were wrung from the King as guarantees against oppression and usurpation of his prerogatives.
Precedent	It did not enter into the minds of the barons to provide security against their own body or in favour of the commons by limiting the power of <ORG>, so that bills of attainder, ex post facto laws, la ws declaring forfeitures of estates and other arbitrary Acts of legisla- tion which occur so frequently in English history, were never regarded as inconsistent with the law of the land, for, notwithstanding what was attributed to Lord <NAME> in.
Precedent	<NAME>'s (1) (1883) 110 U.S. 516.
Precedent	316  case, [8 <NAME> 115, 118 (a),] the omnipotence of <ORG> over the Common Law was absolute, even against common right and reason.
Precedent	The actual and practical security for English liberty against legislative tyranny was the power of a free public opinion represented by the <ORG>.
Precedent	In this country written Constitutions were deemed essen- tial to protect the rights and liberties of the people against the encroachments of power delegated to their gov- ernments and the provisions of Magna Charta were incorporat- ed in the bills of rights.
Precedent	They were limitations upon all the powers of government, legislative as well as executive and judicial."
Precedent	This basic distinction between the two systems should never be lost sight of, if confusion of t hought is to be avoided.
Precedent	Although our Constitution has imposed some limita- tions on the legislative authorities, yet subject to and outside such limitations our Constitution has left our <ORG> and the State <ORG> supreme in their respective legislative fields.
Precedent	In the main, subject to the limitations I have mentioned, our Constitution has preferred the supremacy of the <ORG> to that of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The English principle of due process of law is, therefore, more in accord with our Constitution than the American doctrine which has been evolved for serving quite a differ- ent system.
RatioOfTheDecision	The picturesque language of <NAME> quoted above, while that is quite appropriate to the American Constitution which does not recognise the supremacy of the <ORG>, is wholly out of place in, and has no applica- tion to, a Constitution such as ours, which, subject only to certain restrictions, recognises the supremacy of the Legis- latures in their respective fields.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the next place, it is common knowledge  that our Constitution-makers deliberate- ly declined to adopt the uncertain and shifting American doctrine of due process of law and substituted the words' "except in due process of law" that were in the original draft by the more specific expression "except in accordance with procedure established by law."
RatioOfTheDecision	To try to bring in the American doctrine, in spite of this fact, will be to stulti- fy the intention of the Constitution as expressed in  article 21.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the third place, in view of the plain meaning of the language of that article as construed and explained above it is impossible to let in what have been called the principles of natural justice as adopted in the procedural due process of law by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Again, even the all-pervading little word "due" does not find a place in article 21 so as to qualify the procedure.
RatioOfTheDecision	It speaks of procedure and not "due" procedure and, therefore, "the intellectual yardstick" of the <ORG> is definitely ruled out.
RatioOfTheDecision	Finally, it will be incongruous to import  the doctrine of due process of law without its palliative, the doctrine of police powers.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is impossible to read the last mentioned doctrine into article 21.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has also been suggested as a compromise that this <ORG> should adopt a middle course between the flexible principles of natural justice as adopted by the American doctrine of due process of law and the unbending rigidity of mere <ORG>-made laws.
RatioOfTheDecision	h is said that we have our Code of Criminal Procedure which embodies within its provisions certain salutary principles of procedure and we must insist that those underlying principles should be regarded as procedure established or settled by our positive law.
RatioOfTheDecision	But who will say what are those fundamental principles?
Precedent	What principles.
Precedent	do I reject as inessential and what shall I adopt as fundamental ?
Precedent	What is fundamental to me today may not appear to be so to another Judge a decade hence, for principles give way with changing social conditions.
Precedent	In America it was suggested that due process of law should be taken  to mean the general body of common law as it stood at the date of the Constitution.
Precedent	In <NAME> v. <NAME> (1) it was negatived in the following words:  "'Due process of law' does not mean the general body of the law, common and statute, as it was at the time the Constitution took effect; for that would deny the legisla- ture power to change or amend the law in any particular."
Precedent	The <ORG>, however, brought in principles of (1) 44 Minn. 97.
Precedent	318  natural justice under the due process clause.
Precedent	To sanctify what I may to-day regard as the basic principles underlying our Code of Criminal Procedure will be to make them immuta- ble and to prevent the legislature even to improve upon them.
Precedent	This is nothing but imposing on the legislature a limitation which the Constitution has not placed on it.
Precedent	I do not think it is a permissible adventure for the <ORG> to undertake.
Precedent	It is a dangerous adventure, for it will bring about stagnation which means ruin.
Precedent	We must accept the Con- stitution which is the supreme law.
Precedent	The Constitut ion has by article 21 required a procedure and has prescribed certain minimum requirements of procedure in article 22.
Precedent	To add to them is not to interpret the Constitution but to recast it according to our intellectual yardstick and our unconscious predilections as to what an ideal Constitution should be.
Precedent	Article 21 , in my judgment, only formulates a substan- tive fundamental right to life and personal liberty which in its content is not an absolute right but is a limited right having its ambit circumscribed by the risk of its being taken away by following a procedure established by law made by the appropriate legislative authority and the proximate purpose of article 21 is not to prescribe any particular procedure.
Precedent	It is to be kept in mind that at the date when the Constitution came into effect we had the Indian Penal Code creating diverse offences and a conviction for any of them would deprive a person of his personal liberty.
Precedent	Under article 246 read with Entry 1 of the Concurrent List, Par- liament or any S tate <ORG> could add more offences and create further means for taking away personal liberty.
Precedent	But all this deprivation of personal liberty as a result of a conviction could only be done by following the procedure laid down by the Code of Criminal Procedure.
Precedent	Again, at the date of this Constitution there were preventive detention laws in almost every province and a person could be deprived of his personal liberty under those laws.
Precedent	Those laws, however, provided a procedure of a sort which had to be followed.
Precedent	Therefore, before the Constitution came into force, personal liberty could be taken away only by following the procedure enacted by the Criminal Procedure Code in the case of punitive detention or by the procedure enacted by the different Security Act s in case of preventive detention.
Precedent	Power, however, has been given to <ORG> and the State <ORG>s under article 246 read with Entry 2 of the Concurrent List to make laws with re- spect to Criminal Procedure.
Precedent	If that article stood by itself the Pa <ORG> or <ORG> could repeal the whole of the Criminal Procedure Code and also do away even with the skeleton procedure provided in the Security Act s. If article 246 stood by itself then the appropriate legislative authority could have taken away the life and personal liberty of any person without any procedure at all.
Precedent	This absolute supremacy of the legislative authority has, however, been cut down by article 21 which delimits the ambit and scope of the substantive right to life and person- al liberty by reference to a procedure and by article 22 which prescribes the minimum procedure which must be fol- lowed.
Precedent	In this situation the only power of the <ORG> is to determine whether the impugned law has provided some proce- dure and observed and obeyed the minimum requirements of article 29. and if it has, then it is not for the <ORG> to insist on more elaborate procedure according to its notion or to question the wisdom of the legislative authority in enacting the particular law, however harsh , unreasonable, archaic or odious the provisions of that law may be.
Precedent	It is said that if this strictly technical interpreta- tion is put upon article 21 then it will not constitute a fundamental right at all and need not have been placed in the chapter on Fundamental Rights, for every person's life and personal liberty will be at the mercy of the <ORG> which, by providing some sort of a procedure and complying with the few requirements of article 22 , may, at any time, deprive a person of his life and liberty at its pleasure and whim.
Precedent	There are several answers to this line of argument.
Precedent	Article 21 as construed by me will, if nothing else, cer- tainly protect every person against the executive and as such will be as much a fundamental right deserving  a place in the Constitution as the famous 39th Chapter of the Magna Charta was and is a bulwark of liberty in English law.
Precedent	It appears to me that article 21 of our Constitution read with article 32 also gives us some protection even against the legislative aut hority in that a person may only be deprived of his life and personal liberty in accordance with procedure which, although enacted by it, must at least conform to the requirements of article 22.
Precedent	Subject to this limitation our parliament or <ORG> may enact any law and provide any procedure it pleases for depriving a person of his life and personal liberty under article 21.
Precedent	Such being the meaning of that article and the ambit and extent of the fundamental right of life and personal liberty which the people of this country have given unto themselves, any law for depriving any person of his life and personal liberty that may be made by the appropriate legislative authority under article 246 and in conformity with the requirements of article 22 does not take away or abridge any right conferred by article 21 , for the very right conferred by that article is circumscribed by this possi- bility or risk and, therefore, such law cannot be regarded as violating the provisions of article 13 (2).
Precedent	Our Const itution is a compromise between <ORG>ary supremacy of England and the supremacy of <ORG> of the United States.
Precedent	Subject to the limitations I have mentioned which are certainly justiciable, our Constitution has ac- cepted the supremacy of the legislative authority and, that being so, we must be prepared to face occasional vagaries of that body and to put up with enactments of the nature of the atrocious English statute to which learned counsel for the petitioner has repeatedly referred, namely, that the Bishop of Rochester's cook be boiled to death.
Precedent	If <ORG> may take away life by providing for hanging by the neck, logi- cally there can be no objection if it provides a sentence of death by shooting by a firing squad or by guillotine or in the electric chair or even by boiling in oil.
Precedent	A procedure laid down by the legislature may offend against the <ORG>'s sense of justice and fair play and a sentence provided by the legislature may outrage the <ORG>'s notions of penology, but that is a wholl y irrelevant consideration.
Precedent	The <ORG> may construe and interpret the Constitution and ascertain its true meaning but once that is done the <ORG> cannot question its wisdom or policy.
Precedent	The Constitution is supreme.
Precedent	The <ORG> must take the Constitu- tion as it finds it, even if it does not accord with its preconceived notions of what an ideal Constitution should be.
Precedent	Our protection against legislative tyranny, if any, lies in ultimate analysis in a free and intelligent public opinion which must eventually assert itself.
Precedent	The conclusion I have arrived at does not introduce any novelty, for in many other <ORG> the supremacy of the legislature is recognised in the matter of depriving a person of his life, liberty and property.
Precedent	The English Democratic Constitution is one in point.
Precedent	Take the Constitu- tion of the Irish Free State.
Precedent	Article 40 (4) (i) provides that no citizen shall be deprived of personal liberty save in accordance with law, and article 50 (5) guarantees that the dwelling of every citizen is inviola ble and shall not be forcibly entered save in accordance with law.
Precedent	The words "in accordance with law" in both the above clauses must mean the same thing and I have no doubt in my mind, reading clause (5)that it means in accordance with the <ORG>-made law, for we have not been referred to any rule prescribed by natural justice regulating searches of, or entry into, dwelling houses.
Precedent	Article 107 (2) of the Czechoslovakian Constitution uses the words "in accordance with law" which, read with clause (1) of that article, obviously means the law to be made which will form part of the Constitution.
Precedent	Take the Constitution of the Free City of Danzig.
Precedent	Article74 of that Constitution which is in Part II headed "Fundamental Bights and Duties" provides as follows:  "The liberty of the person shall be inviolable.
Precedent	No limitation or deprivation of personal liberty may be imposed by public authority, except by virtue of a law."
Precedent	322  The word" law" clearly cannot, in the context,mcan princi- ples of natural justice- Again, articl e 75 of that Consti- tution protects the freedom of movement within the Free City and the right to stay and to settle at any place, to acquire real property and to earn a living.
Precedent	It concludes by saying that this right shall not be curtailed without legal sanctions.
Precedent	Legal sanctions, in this context, can only mean sanctions of the City laws.
Precedent	Article 114 of the Weimar Constitution is on the same lines and expressed in almost the same language as article 74 of the Danzig Constitution.
Precedent	Take the Japanese Constitution of 1946 from which our arti- cle 21 is reputed to have been taken.
Precedent	Article XXXI of that Constitution says:  No person shall be deprived of life or liberty nor shall any other criminal penalty be imposed, except according to procedure established by law."
Precedent	Surely the words "except according to procedure established by law" in their application to the imposition of criminal penalty must mean State-made law and the same words in the same sentence in the same article cannot, according to ordi- nary rules o f construction of statutes, mean a different thing in their application to deprivation of life or liber- ty.
Precedent	I am aware that it is not right to construe one Consti- tution in the light of another and that is not my purpose when I refer to the other Constitutions; but I do think that after reading the relevant provisions of other written Con- stitutions one sees quite clearly that there is no pressing special reason applicable to or inherent in written Consti- tutions which requires the importation of the principles of natural justice or of the American doctrine of due process of law into our Constitution.
Precedent	The several Constitutions referred to above have not adopted that American doctrine but have been content with leaving the life and liberty of their citizens to the care of the laws made by their legis- latures.
Precedent	It is no novelty if our Constitution has done the same.
Precedent	For all these reasons, in spite of the very able and attractive arguments of the learned counsel for the peti- tioner which I freely acknowledg e, I am not convinced that there is any scope for the introduction into article 21 of our Constitution of the doctrine of due process of law even as regards procedure.
Precedent	I may or may not like it, but that is the result of our Constitution as I understand it.
Precedent	The learned Attorney-General has referred to certain debates in <ORG> on the original clause which has now become article 21 , not as evidence to be used in interpreting the language of article 21 but as disclos- ing the historical background.
Precedent	His purpose, he says, is to show that the framers of our Constitution had the essential difference in the meaning of the phrases "due process of law" and "according to procedure established by law" clearly explained to them, that they knew that the former implied the supremacy of the judiciary and the latter the supremacy of the legislature and with all that knowledge they deliber- ately agreed to reject the former expression and adopt the latter.
Precedent	As, in my opinion, it is possible to interpret the l anguage of article 21 on the ordinary rules of interpreta- tion of statutes, I do not think it is at all necessary to refer to the debates.
Precedent	As I do not propose to refer to, or rely on, the debates for the purposes of this case, I express no opinion on the question of the admissibility or otherwise of the debates.
Precedent	I now pass on to article 22.
Precedent	The contention of learned counsel for the petitioner is that article 21 by reason of the last few words, "according to procedure established by law" attracts the four requirements of the American proce- dural due process of law as summarised by <NAME> to which reference has been made earlier, and that those require- ments, except to the extent they have been expressly abro- gated or modified by article 22 , must be strictly followed before a person may be deprived of his life or personal liberties.
Precedent	I have already stated for reasons set forth above, that there is no scope for introducing any rule of natural justice or the American procedural due process of law or any unde rlying principle of our Code of Criminal Procedure into that article.
Precedent	This being the conclusion I have arrived at, the major premise assumed by learned coun- sel for the petitioner is missing and this line of argument does not begin and cannot be accepted.
Precedent	The learned Attorney-General, on the other hand.
Precedent	has at one stage of his argument, urged that article 21 has nothing to do with preventive detention at all and that preventive detention is wholly covered by article 22 (4) to (7) which by themselves constitute a complete code.
Precedent	I am unable to accede to this extreme point of view also.
Precedent	The true posi- tion, as I apprehend it, lies between the two extreme views.
Precedent	Article 21 , to my mind, gives protection to life and person- al liberty to the extent therein mentioned.
Precedent	It does not recognise the right to life and personal liberty as an absolute right but delimits the ambit and scope of the right itself The absolute right is by the definition in that article cut down by the risk of its being taken away in accordance  with procedure established by law.
Precedent	It is this circumscribed right which is substantively protected by article 21 as against the executive as well as the legislature, for the Constitution has conditioned its depri- vation by the necessity for a procedure established by law made by itself.
Precedent	While subclauses (2) to (6) of article 19 have put a limit on the fundamental rights of a citizen, articles 21 and 22 have put a limit on the power of the <ORG> given under article 246 read with the legislative lists.
Precedent	Under our Constitution our life and personal liberty are balanced by restrictions on the rights of the citizens as laid down in article 19 and by the checks put upon the <ORG> by articles 21 and 22. preventive detention deprives a person of his personal liberty as effectively as does punitive detention and, therefore, personal liberty, circum- scribed as it is by the risk of its being taken away, re- quires protection against punitive as well as preventive detention.
Precedent	The language of article 21 is quite general a nd is wide enough to give its limited protection to personal liberty against all forms of detention.
Precedent	It protects a person against preventive detention by the executive without the sanction of a law made by the legislature.
Precedent	It prevents the legislature from taking away a person's personal liberty except in accordance with procedure established by law, although such law is to be by itself.
Precedent	If, as contended by the learned Attorney-General and held by me, article 19 only protects the rights of a free citizen as long as he is free and does not deal with total deprivation of personal liberty and if, as contended by the learned Attorney-General, article 21 does not protect a person against preventive detention then where is the protection for life and personal liberty as substantive rights which the procedural provisions of arti- cle 22 may protect ?
Precedent	What is the use of procedural protec- tion if there is no substantive right ?
Precedent	In my judgment article 21 protects the substantive rights by requiring a procedure and arti cle 22 gives the minimum procedural pro- tection.
Precedent	Clauses (1) and (2) of article 22 lay down the procedure that has to be followed when a man is arrested.
Precedent	They ensure four things: (a) right to be informed regarding grounds of arrest, (b) right to consult, and to be defended by, a legal practitioner of his choice, (c) right to be produced before a magistrate within 24 hours and (d) freedom from detention beyond the said period except by order of the magis- trate.
Precedent	These four procedural requirements are very much similar to the requirements of the procedural due process of law as enumerated by <NAME>.
Precedent	Some of these salutary protections are also to be found in our Code of Criminal Procedure.
Precedent	If the procedure has already been prescribed by article 21 incorporating the principles of natural justice or the principles underlying our Code of Criminal Procedure what was the necessity of repeating them in clauses (1) and (2) of article 22 ?
Precedent	Why this unnecessary overlapping ?
Precedent	The truth is that article 21 does not prescr ibe any particular procedure but in defining the protection to life and person- al liberty merely envisages or indicates the necessity for a procedure and article 22 lays down the minimum rules of procedure that even <ORG> cannot abrogate or overlook.
Precedent	This is so far as punitive detention is concerned.
Precedent	But clause (3) of article 22 expressly provides that none of the procedure laid down in clauses (1) and (2) shall apply to an alien enemy or to a person who is arrested or detained under any law providing for preventive detention.
Precedent	It is thus expressly made clear that a detenu need not be produced before the magistrate and he is not to have the assistance of any lawyer for consultation or for defending him.
Precedent	Such being the express provision of our Constitution nobody can question its wisdom.
Precedent	So I pass on.
Precedent	Clauses (4), (5), (6) and (7) of article 22 in terms relate to preventive detention.
Precedent	Article 246 authorises the appropriate legislature to make a law for preventive deten- tion in terms of Entry 9 in List I  and/or Entry 3 in List III of the Seventh Schedule.
Precedent	On this legislative power are imposed certain limitations by article 22 (4) to (7).
Precedent	According to this the legislature, whether it be <ORG> or <ORG>, is reminded that no law made by it for preventive detention shall authorise the detention of a person for a longer period than three months except in two cases mentioned in sub-clauses (a) and (b).
Precedent	The proviso to sub-clause (a) and sub-clause (b) refer to a law made only by <ORG> under clause (7).
Precedent	Under clause (7) it is <ORG> alone and not any State <ORG> that may prescribe what are specified in the three subclauses of that clause.
Precedent	Although <ORG> may make a law for preventive detention in terms of Entry 3 in List III of the Seventh Schedule no such law may authorise detention for more than three months unless the provisions of sub-clauses  (a)and (b) of clause (4) sanction such detention.
Precedent	Even a law made by <ORG> cannot authorise detention for more than thre e months unless it is a law made under the provi- sions of clause (7).
Precedent	In short, clause (4) of article 22 provides a limitation on the legislative power as to the period of preventive detention.
Precedent	Apart from imposing a limitation on the legislative power, clause (4) also pre- scribes a procedure of detention for a period longer than three months by providing for an advisory board.
Precedent	Then comes clause (5).
Precedent	It lays down the procedure that has to be fol- lowed when a person is detained under any law providing for preventive detention, namely, (a) the grounds of the order of detention must be communicated to the detenu as soon as may be, and (b) the detenu must be afforded the earliest opportunity of making a representation against the order.
Precedent	The first requirement takes the place of notice and the second that of a defence or hearing.
Precedent	These are the only compulsory procedural requirements laid down by our Constitution.
Precedent	There is nothing to prevent the <ORG> from providing an elaborate procedure regulating preventi ve detention but it is not obliged to do so.
Precedent	If some procedure is provided as envisaged by article 21 and the compulsory requirements of article 22 are obeyed and carried out nobody can, under our Constitution, as I read it, complain of the law providing for preventive detention.
Argument	Learned counsel for the petitioner concedes that the four requirements of procedural due process summarised by <NAME> will have to be modified in their application to preventive detention.
Argument	Thus he does not insist on a prior notice before arrest, for he recognises that such a require- ment may frustrate the very object of preventive detention by giving an opportunity to the person in question to go underground.
Argument	The provision in clause (5) for supplying grounds is a good substitute for notice.
Argument	He also does not insist that the <ORG> to judge the reasonableness of the detention should be a judicial tribunal.
Argument	He will be satis- fied if the tribunal or advisory board, as it is called in article 22 of the Constitution, is an impartial bo dy and goes into the merits of the order of detention and its decision is binding on the executive government.
Argument	He insists that the detenu must have a reasonable and effective oppor- tunity to put up his defence.
Argument	He does not insist on the assistance of counsel, for that is expressly taken away by the Constitution itself.
Argument	But he insists on what he calls an effective opportunity of being heard in person before an impartial tribunal which will be free to examine the grounds of his detention and whose decision should be binding alike on the detenu and the executive authority which detains.
Precedent	The claim may be reasonable but the question before the <ORG> is not reasonableness or otherwise of the provisions of article 22 (4) to (7).
Precedent	Those provisions are not justicia- ble, for they are the provisions of the Constitution itself which is supreme over everybody.
Precedent	42  328  The <ORG> can only seek to find out, on a proper construc- tion, what protection has in fact been provided.
Precedent	The Consti- tution has provided for the givin g of the grounds of deten- tion although facts as distinguished from grounds may be withheld under clause (6) and the right of representation against the order of detention.
Precedent	It has provided for the duration of the detention.
Precedent	There the guaranteed fundamental procedural rights end.
Precedent	There is no provision for any trial before any tribunal.
Precedent	One cannot import the condition of a trial by any tribunal from the fact that a right of repre- sentation has been given.
Precedent	The right to make representation is nothing more than the right to "lodge objections" as provided by the Danzig Constitution and the Weimar Constitu- tion.
Precedent	The representations made will no doubt be considered by the Government.
Precedent	It is said a prosecutor cannot be himself the judge.
Precedent	Ordinarily, the orders of detention will in a great majority of cases be made by the District Magistrate or Sub-Divisional Officer or the Commissioner of Police.
Precedent	The representation of the detenu goes to the Government.
Precedent	Why should it be assumed that a high government official at the  seat of the government will not impartially consider the representation and judge the propriety of the order of detention made by local officials ?
Precedent	Clause (5) does not imperatively provide for any oral representation which a hearing will entail.
Precedent	Indeed the exclusion of the provisions of clauses (1) and (2) negatives any idea of trial or oral defence.
Precedent	The <ORG> may not, by temperament and training, like this at all but it cannot question the wisdom or the policy of the Constitution.
Precedent	In my judgment as regards pre- ventive detention laws, the only limitation put upon the legislative power is that it must provide some procedure and at least incorporate the minimum requirements laid down in article 22 (4) to (7).
Precedent	There is no limitation as regards the substantive law.
Precedent	Therefore, a preventive detention law which provides some procedure and complies with the require- ments of article 22 (4) to (7) must be held to be a good law, however odious it may appear to the <ORG> to be.
Precedent	329  Learned counsel for the petitioner  contends -that the impugned Act does not comply with even the bare requirements of article 22 (4) to (7).
Precedent	It is pointed out that section 3 of the Act does not lay down any objective test but leaves it to the authority to define and say whether a particular person comes within the legislative heads.
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words, it is contended that <ORG> has not legislated at all but has delegated its legislative powers to the executive authorities.
RatioOfTheDecision	I do not think there is any substance in this contention.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the first place this is not an objection as to procedure but to substantive law which is not open to the <ORG>'s scrutiny.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the next place this contention over- looks the basic distinction between the delegation of power to make the law and the conferring of an authority and discretion as to its execution to be exercised under and in pursuance of the law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The impugned Act has specifically set forth an ascertainable standard by which the conduct of a particular person is to be judged by the detaining authori- ty .
RatioOfTheDecision	It is next urged that section 12 of the Act does not comply with the requirements of clause (7) of article 22 for two reasons, namely--  (i) that clause (7) contemplates a law prescribing the circumstances under which, and the class or classes of cases in which, a person may be detained for a period longer than three months and then another law thereafter providing for preventive detention for a period longer than three months; and  (ii) that under clause (7) <ORG> must prescribe both the circumstances under which, and the class or classes of cases in which, a person may be detained for a period longer than three months.
RatioOfTheDecision	As regards the first point I do not see why <ORG> must make two laws, one laying down the principles for longer detention and another for detention for such longer period.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be that a <ORG> cannot provide for longer detention until <ORG> has made the law, but I can see no reason why <ORG> cannot do both by the same Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	In fact, clause (4) (b) contemplates the det ention itself to be in accordance with the provisions of any law made by <ORG> under sub- clauses (a) and (b) of clause (7).
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, the detention can well be under the very law which the <ORG> makes under sub-clauses (a) and (b) of clause (7).
Precedent	As to the second point the argument is that <ORG> has a discre- tion under clause (7) to make a law and it is not obliged to make any law but when our <ORG> chooses to make a law it must prescribe both the circumstances under which, and the class or classes of cases in which, a person may be detained for a period longer than three months.
Precedent	I am unable to construe clause (7) (a) in the way suggested by learned counsel for the petitioner.
Precedent	It is an enabling provision empowering <ORG> to prescribe two things.
Precedent	<ORG> may prescribe either or both.
Precedent	H a <NAME> tells his delicate child that he may play table tennis and badminton but not the strenuous game of football, it obviously does not mean that <NAME>, if he chooses to play at all, must pl ay both table tennis and badminton.
Precedent	It is an option given to the child.
Precedent	Likewise, the Constitution gives to <ORG> the power of prescribing two things.
Precedent	<ORG> is not obliged to prescribe at all but if it chooses to prescribe it may prescribe either or both.
Precedent	Clause 7 (a), in my opinion, has to be read distributively as follows: The <ORG> may prescribe the circumstance under which a person may be detained for a period longer than three months and Parlia- ment may prescribe the class or classes of cases in which a person may be detained for a period longer than three months.
Precedent	That appears to me to be consonant with sound rules of construction.
Precedent	Further, the circumstances and the class or classes of cases may conceivably coalesce.
Precedent	Indeed the <ORG> case No.
Precedent	1 of 1950 before <ORG> (<NAME> v. The Chief Secretary) itself indicates that the same provision may be read as circumstances or as a classification.
Precedent	In that case learned counsel conceded that section 12 had prescrib ed the circumstances but his com- plaint was that it had not prescribed the class or classes of cases.
Precedent	The majority of the <ORG> repelled this contention.
Precedent	One learned Judge howev- er, held that section 12 had prescribed the class or classes of cases but had not prescribed the circumstances.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is, therefore, clear that the classification itself may indicate the circumstances.
RatioOfTheDecision	Again, the classification may be on a variety of bases.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be according to provinces the detenus come from.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be according to the age of the detenus.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be according to the object they are supposed to have in view or according to the activities they are suspected to be engaged in.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this case <ORG> has taken five out of the six legislative heads and divided them into two categories.
RatioOfTheDecision	The detenus are thus classified ac- cording to their suspected object or activities endangering the several matters specified in the section.
RatioOfTheDecision	I do not see why classification cannot be made on the footing of the objectives of the detenus fal ling in some of the legislative heads, for each legislative head has a specific connotation well understood in law.
RatioOfTheDecision	If I am correct that there has been a classification then the fact that a person falls within one or the other class may well be the circumstances under which he may be detained for a period longer than three months.
RatioOfTheDecision	I do not consider it right, as a matter of con- struction, to read any further limitation in clause 7 (a) of article 22.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my judgment Par]lament was not obliged under clause (7) to prescribe both circumstances and classes, and in any case has in fact and substance prescribed both.
RatioOfTheDecision	I am conscious that a law made by <ORG> under article 22 (7) will do away with the salutary safeguard of the opinion of an advisory board.
RatioOfTheDecision	But it must be remembered that our Constitution itself contemplates that in certain circumstances or for certain class or classes of detenus even the advisory board may not be safe and it has trusted <ORG> to make a law for that purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	Our preference for a n advisory board should not blind us to this aspect of the matter.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is true that circumstances ordinarily relate to extraneous things, like riots, commotion, political or communal or some sort of abnormal situation and it is said that the framers of the Constitution had in mind some such situation when <ORG> might be done away with.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also urged that they had in mind that the more dangerous types of detenus should be denied the privi- lege of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	I am free to confess that prescription of specific circumstances or a more rigid and definite specification of classes would have been better and more desirable.
RatioOfTheDecision	But that is crying for the ideal.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution has not in terms put any such limitation as regards the circumstances or the class or classes of cases and it is idle to speculate as to the intention of the Constitution-makers, who, by the way, are the very persons who made this law.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not for the <ORG> to improve upon or add to the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the law duly made  by Parlia- ment is repugnant to good sense, public opinion will compel <ORG> to alter it suitably.
RatioOfTheDecision	Finally, an objection is taken that section 14 of the impugned Act takes away or abridges the right of the detenu to move this <ORG> by appropriate proceedings.
RatioOfTheDecision	Both clauses (1) and (2) of article 32 speak of enforcement of rights conferred by Part III.
RatioOfTheDecision	The right to move this <ORG> is given to a person not for the sake of moving only but for moving the <ORG> for the enforcement of some rights conferred by Part III and this <ORG> has been given power to issue direc- tions or orders or writs for the enforcement of any of such rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	In order, therefore, to attract the application of article 32 , the person applying must first satisfy that he has got a right under Part III which has to be enforced under article 32.
RatioOfTheDecision	I have already said that article 19 does not deal with the freedom of the person.
RatioOfTheDecision	I have also said that articles 21 and 22 provide for protection by insisting on some procedure.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under article 22 ( 5) the authority making the order of detention is enjoined, as soon as may be, to communicate to the detenu the grounds on which that order has been made.
RatioOfTheDecision	This provision has some purpose, name- ly, that the disclosure of the grounds will afford the detenu the opportunity of making a representation against the order.
RatioOfTheDecision	Supposing the authority does not give any grounds at all as distinct from facts referred to in Clause (6).
RatioOfTheDecision	Surely, the detenu loses a fundamental right because he is prevented from making a representation against the order.
RatioOfTheDecision	of deten- tion.
RatioOfTheDecision	Suppose the authority hands over to the detenu a piece of paper with some scribblings on it which do not amount to any ground at all for detention.
RatioOfTheDecision	Then also the detenu can legitimately complain that his right has been infringed.
RatioOfTheDecision	He can then come to the <ORG> to get redress under article 32 , but he cannot show to the <ORG> the piece of paper with the scribblings on it under section 14 of the Act and the <ORG> cannot judge whether he has actually got the grounds  which he is entitled to under article 22 (5).
Precedent	In.
Precedent	such a case the <ORG> may well complain that both his substantive right under article 22 (5)' as well as his right to constitutional remedies under article 32 have been in- fringed.
Precedent	He can complain of infringement of his remedial rights under article 32 , because he cannot show that there has been an infringement of his substantive right under article 22 (5).
RatioOfTheDecision	It appears to me, therefore, that section 14 of the Act in so far as it prevents the <ORG> from disclosing to the <ORG> the grounds communicated to him is not in conformity with Part III of the Constitution and is, therefore, void under article 13 (2).
RatioOfTheDecision	That section, howev- er, is clearly severable and cannot affect the whole Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	On this question the views of <NAME> and <NAME> of Patna in Criminal Miscellaneous No.
RatioOfTheDecision	124 of 1950 (<NAME> v. The <ORG>) and the majority of the learned Judges of the Calcutta High <ORG> in Full Bench Case No.
RatioOfTheDecision	1 of 1950 (<NAME> v. <ORG> tary) appear to be correct and sound.
RatioOfTheDecision	For the reasons I have given above, in my opinion, the impugned Act is a valid law except as to section 14 in so far as it prevents the grounds being disclosed to the Court.
RulingByPresentCourt	The petitioner before us does not complain that he has not got proper grounds.
RulingByPresentCourt	Further, the period of his detention under the impugned Act has not gone beyond three months and, in the circumstances, this application should, in my opinion, stand dismissed.
RulingByPresentCourt	Petition dismissed.
